Jerome Powell Decides to Stay on the Federal Reserve Board of Governors

Wall Street Journal Opinion(136.0K) ·27:08 ·2.0K views · 2026-04-30
Jerome Powell (Outgoing Chair, Federal Reserve) Kevin Warsh (Federal Reserve)
Kevin Warsh is set to become Federal Reserve Chairman amid an AI-driven but inflationary economy, while outgoing Chair Jerome Powell’s unprecedented decision to remain on the Board of Governors creates a complex leadership dynamic that limits the Tru...
TL;DR Kevin Warsh is set to become Federal Reserve Chairman amid an AI-driven but inflationary economy, while outgoing Chair Jerome Powell’s unprecedented decision to remain on the Board of Governors creates a complex leadership dynamic that limits the Trump administration’s...

TL;DR

Kevin Warsh is set to become Federal Reserve Chairman amid an AI-driven but inflationary economy, while outgoing Chair Jerome Powell’s unprecedented decision to remain on the Board of Governors creates a complex leadership dynamic that limits the Trump administration’s ability to appoint a new majority.

Economic Stagnation vs. The AI Investment Boom

The U.S. economy shows modest growth with underlying "stagflationary" signals as Kevin Warsh prepares to take office.

  • GDP Trends: First-quarter GDP grew at 2%, an increase from the previous quarter’s 0.5%, but remains far from a "boom."
  • The AI Rescue: Investment in AI is currently the primary driver of growth.
      • Capital Expenditure: Big Tech firms (Meta, Microsoft, Google, Amazon) spent $130 billion on AI in Q1 alone, with annual spending projected to reach $700 billion.
      • Growth Concentration: Growth is heavily concentrated in IT equipment and intellectual property; without this "AI boom," the broader economy would likely be flat or mediocre.
  • Consumer Weakness: Consumer spending is cooling, with much of the current expenditure driven by necessities like healthcare and rising gasoline prices rather than discretionary confidence.

Persistent Inflation and Tariff Impacts

Despite the Federal Reserve's previous efforts, inflation remains significantly above the 2% target, complicating the transition to the Warsh era.

  • PCE Data: Personal Consumption Expenditure (PCE) inflation is at 4.3%–4.5% year-over-year, suggesting the Fed may have been premature in previous interest rate cuts.
  • Tariff Pressure: Significant price increases are appearing in goods subject to tariffs:
      • Recreational vehicles: Up 20.4%
      • Clothing and footwear: Up 6.8%
      • Household furnishings: Up 5.4%
  • Policy Miscalculation: Joe Sternberg notes the Fed has been "unduly optimistic" regarding inflation returning to target, failing to account for the "roller coaster" effect similar to the 1970s.

The Powell-Warsh Leadership Transition

Jerome Powell’s decision to stay on the Board of Governors after his chairmanship ends in May is a major historical anomaly with significant political implications.

  • Historical Rarity: Powell will be the first Fed Chair in 78 years to remain on the board as a governor after stepping down from the top post.
  • The DOJ Probe: Powell cites a recently closed Department of Justice criminal investigation into Fed reconstruction costs as his reason for staying, seeking "finality and transparency."
  • Political Strategy: By remaining on the board, Powell denies President Trump the opportunity to appoint a new governor, preventing the administration from securing an immediate majority on the seven-member board.
  • Board Dynamics: While Powell claims he will maintain a "low profile," his presence—and his deep connections to the press—may create an "over the shoulder" environment for Warsh.

Internal Fed Friction and Reform Agenda

Kevin Warsh inherits a Federal Reserve that is seeing increased internal dissent and a push for structural reform.

  • Regional Dissent: Three regional bank presidents recently dissented from the FOMC statement, objecting to "dovish" language—a signal that Warsh may face pressure to maintain a hawkish stance.
  • Warsh’s Philosophy: Warsh has expressed a desire for "messy meetings" with more open debate and less reliance on the consensus-driven models used by the current staff.
  • Structural Goals: Warsh is expected to push for a return to "classic" open market operations, moving away from the era of massive bond-buying (quantitative easing) that characterized the Bernanke and Powell eras.

Notable Quotes

"I’ve said that I will not leave the board until this investigation is well and truly over with transparency and finality, and I stand by that." — Jerome Powell, Outgoing Fed Chair
"If you connect the dots, this is what I would call an AI boom... it is an AI-driven economy, I would say, at this point." — Allysia Finley, WSJ Editorial Board
"The reality is the American households are now five years or more into this cycle of rapidly escalating prices... the Fed’s predictions for what inflation would do in the future have always turned out to be wrong." — Joe Sternberg, WSJ Editorial Board
"By Powell staying on, he's essentially denying Trump a majority on the board." — Allysia Finley, WSJ Editorial Board

Robotics' End Game: Nvidia's Jim Fan

Sequoia Capital(163.0K) ·20:03 ·26.5K views · 2026-04-30
Jim Fan (Lead, Embodied Autonomous Research Group, NVIDIA)
Jim Fan argues that robotics is undergoing "The Great Parallel" to the LLM playbook, transitioning from language-heavy models to World Action Models (WAM) trained on massive egocentric video datasets and neural simulations, aiming to pass the physical Turing test within 2–3 years.
TL;DR Jim Fan argues that robotics is undergoing "The Great Parallel" to the LLM playbook, transitioning from language-heavy models to World Action Models (WAM) trained on massive egocentric video datasets and neural simulations, aiming to pass the physical Turing test within...

TL;DR

Jim Fan argues that robotics is undergoing "The Great Parallel" to the LLM playbook, transitioning from language-heavy models to World Action Models (WAM) trained on massive egocentric video datasets and neural simulations, aiming to pass the physical Turing test within 2–3 years.

The Great Parallel: Copying the LLM Success

Jim Fan proposes that robotics is currently "speedrunning" the same four-stage evolution that defined Large Language Models (LLMs) between 2020 and 2026.

  • The Four Stages:
      • Pre-training (2020/GPT-3): Learning the "grammar" of the world; for robots, this is simulating the next physical state.
      • Alignment/Fine-tuning (2022/InstructGPT): Shaping the simulation to perform useful, specific work.
      • Reasoning (2024/o1): Using Reinforcement Learning (RL) to surpass simple imitation learning.
      • AutoResearch (2026/Expected): Accelerating the loop through autonomous agents and swarm collaboration.
  • The Shift to World Action Models (WAM): Fan argues that current Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models are "head-heavy" on language but "physics-light" on verbs and spatial reasoning.
      • DreamZero: Nvidia’s new model that "dreams" a few seconds into the future, decoding next world states and motor actions simultaneously to solve zero-shot tasks.
      • Physics Emergence: By predicting pixels at scale, models like Veo-3 naturally learn gravity, buoyancy, and visual planning without explicit coding.

Scaling Data: From Tele-op to Egocentric Video

The "bottleneck" in robotics is the data flywheel. Fan identifies a shift away from traditional, non-scalable collection methods.

  • The Death of Tele-operation: Traditional tele-op is capped by the 24-hour physical limit of a robot/human pair and is often inefficient and slow.
  • Sensorized Human Data:
      • Universal Manipulation Interface (UMI): Wearable robot actuators that allow humans to collect data without the robot body in the loop.
      • DexUMI: An exoskeleton designed by Nvidia that maps human hand movements to 5-finger dexterous robot hands, enabling 1:1 data collection.
  • EgoScale (The FSD Equivalent): This model uses human egocentric (POV) videos as its primary "diet."
      • Scalability: Nvidia pre-trained EgoScale on 21,000 hours of human video with 0% robot data, using only 4 hours of tele-op for final alignment.
      • The Scaling Law: Fan identified a clean log-linear relationship between pre-training hours and validation loss for dexterity, similar to language model scaling laws.

Environments as Data: Neural Simulation

To reach the scale of 1 million environments, robotics must move beyond physical hardware into digital space.

  • The Equation: Fan asserts that "Compute = Environment = Data." The more compute available for simulation, the more data the model generates autonomously.
  • Real-to-Sim-to-Real: Using consumer devices like iPhones as "pocket world scanners" to turn physical objects into interactive 3D assets inside physics simulators.
  • DreamDojo (Neural Simulators): A purely data-driven simulator that takes actions as input and outputs RGB frames and sensor states.
      • It bypasses classical physics equations and graphics engines entirely, learning the "mechanics" of a robot from data.

The Robotics Roadmap to 2040

Fan outlines three major achievements remaining on the "technology tree" for robotics to reach its endgame.

  • The Physical Turing Test (2–3 Years): Reaching a state where a robot's labor is indistinguishable from a human’s in terms of energy in vs. unit labor out.
  • The Physical API: Creating standardized command-line interfaces for robot fleets, enabling "lights-out factories" that function as "printers of atoms" to output fully assembled products from markdown files.
  • Physical AutoResearch (By 2040): The final stage where robots design and build the next generation of themselves, far surpassing human capabilities.
"The main diet for robotics will be egocentric videos... We're able to break the curse of 24 hours per robot per day." — Jim Fan
"Our generation was born too late to explore the earth and too early to explore the stars, but we're born just in time to solve robotics." — Jim Fan
"If you believe in robotics, robotics will believe in you." — Jim Fan (referencing Ilya Sutskever)
And up first, I'm delighted to introduce my friend Jim Fan. Jim leads the embodied autonomous research group at Nvidia, otherwise known as Nvidia Robotics. I think that robots are just one of the most thrilling things that's going to happen. A car basically is a big robot, but I'm excited for robots that can go "beep boop" and lift things for us. Jim was a standout at last year's AI Ascent, and we're delighted to have you back. Thanks everyone, thanks. So it was a summer day in 2016, actually right in this office that we're sitting in. There's a guy in a shiny leather jacket, you know, big biceps, hurling in this large metal tray. On this large piece of metal, he wrote, "To Elon and the OpenAI team, to the future of computing and humanity, I present you the world's first DGX-1." That was the first time I met Jensen. And as any good intern would do, I rushed to get in line to sign my name on it. So can you spot it, my name? It's here. And can you spot another? That's Andre right there. So Andre, we're going to the computer history museum. I feel like a dinosaur. You know back then, I had no clue what I was signing up for. And then no one can describe what happened next better than Ilya himself: "If you believe in deep learning, deep learning will believe in you." And oh boy, did deep learning believe in all of us big time. Three step functions, six years—that's how it all took to bring us here today. The first tick: GPT-3, 2020, pre-training. Next token prediction is really about learning the rules of grammar, the shape of language. It's about simulating how thoughts and code and strings in general should unfold. 2022: InstructGPT, supervised fine-tuning, aligning this simulation to do useful work. OpenAI o1, 2024: reasoning using reinforcement learning to surpass imitation learning. And finally, AutoResearch, 2026: accelerating the whole loop beyond what's humanly possible. So as Andre said, all the labs are getting to the final boss battle. It's a lot more complex at scale, of course—you don't just have a single train.py file to tune. But doing it is "just engineering" and it's going to work. You spin up a swarm of agents, you have them collaborate to tune smaller models, you promote the most promising ideas to increasingly larger scales, and humans contribute on the edges. So for LLMs, they are in the thick of the endgame. And honestly, I'm very jealous. Look at how happy Andre was, big smile on his face. The LLM folks are having the party of their lifetime. They're speedrunning AGI on mythical creatures literally called mistrals. So why can't robotics get a piece of that fun? So as any self-respecting scientist would do, I copy homework and I give it a new name. I call it "The Great Parallel." So instead of simulating strings, can we simulate the next physical world state? And then we could align through action fine-tuning onto a thin slice of that simulation that matters for real robots. And we let reinforcement learning carry the last mile. And that's it. "The Great Parallel," copying the LLM success. If you can't beat them, join them. So please join me in a new episode: "Robotics: The Endgame." And sorry, I just couldn't resist. Nanobananas are too good. Thanks, Demis. So how do we play the endgame? It boils down to two things: model strategy and data strategy. Let's look at the model first. The last three years were dominated by VLAs or vision language action models. Models like GROOT and Pi fall in this category. So we assume that the pre-training is done by a VLM, and we simply graft an action head on top of it. But really, if you think about these models, they are LVAs: language is greater than vision is greater than action because the most amount of parameters are dedicated to language. So language is the first-class citizen followed by vision and action. And by design, VLAs are great at encoding knowledge and nouns, but not so much at physics and verbs. It's kind of head-heavy in the wrong places. This is my favorite example from the original VLA paper: "Move Coke can to Taylor Swift." Yes, it has not seen Taylor Swift before. Yes, it's able to generalize. But this is not quite the pre-training ability that we're looking for. So what's the second pre-training paradigm? And I always thought that it would be something glorious. Unfortunately, it turns out that this is AI video slop, as we call it. You know, I can watch these cats playing banjo on security cam all day. It's peak internet. But really, look at this. No one can take this seriously until we realize that these video models are learning to simulate the next world state internally. So these are some rollouts from Veo-3. You can see that the models pick up gravity, buoyancy, lighting, reflection, and refraction, all by themselves. None of this is coded in. Physics emerge by predicting the next blob of pixels at scale. And even visual planning emerges. Look at how Veo solves these mazes. It solves them by running simulation forward in pixel space. And draw attention to the lower right corner here. This is my favorite example. Let's watch, and you blink if you miss, how Veo-3 solves this one. It's super smart. You know, Veo-3 figures out that if you're not looking, geometry is optional. I call this physics slop. So how do we make these world models useful? Well, we do action fine-tuning. We align this superposition of all possible future states and collapse that onto a thin slice that matters for real robots. Introducing DreamZero. It's a new type of policy model that dreams a couple seconds into the future and acts accordingly. And you know that motor actions, they are high-dimensional continuous signals. So that looks just like pixels. We can render it at the same time as we render the videos. So DreamZero jointly decodes the next world states and next actions. And as a result, it's able to zero-shot solve tasks and verbs that it has never seen in training. And as the robot executes, we can visualize what it is dreaming about. And the correlation is very tight. If the video prediction works, the action works. If the video hallucinates, the action fails. So once again, vision and action are now first-class citizens. And we have a lot of fun with DreamZero. So we just roll the robot around in our lab and then type random things into the prompt box. And of course, DreamZero is not going to get all of these tasks 100% robust, but it's kind of like GPT-2. It's trying to get the shape of the motion correct in every case. DreamZero is our first step towards open-ended, open-vocabulary prompting for robotics. And we call this new type of model "World Action Models" or WAM. So let's all take a moment of silence for our dear friend VLA. They've served us well. Rest in peace. Long live world action models. And next, data strategy. This is Nvidia's chief scientist Bill Dally operating tele-operation inside our lab. Given his salary, I think this is by far the most expensive tele-op trajectory ever collected in our dataset. The past three years have been dominated by tele-operation. It's the golden era, right? VR headsets, extremely optimized latency for streaming, and these complex rigs that look like medieval torture devices. You know, so much investment in industry, so much pain and suffering. And yet for tele-op, it's upper-bounded by 24 hours per robot per day. That's the fundamental physical limit. And actually, who am I kidding? It's more like 3 hours per robot per day, and only when the robot god is merciful, because they throw tantrums all the time. So how can we do better? Well, how about this? You just wear the robot hand on your own hand. So this is called UMI, or Universal Manipulation Interface, and it's a deceptively simple idea. You wear the robot actuator on your hand and directly collect the data as humans, while getting the rest of the robot body out of the loop. Yet I would say UMI is perhaps one of the greatest papers ever written in robotics data, and it spawned two unicorn startups. On the left-hand side is Generalist, improving this design so you can wear the gripper here. And then on the right-hand side, Sunday made these three-finger data gloves. So last year, we took it one step further. We designed this exoskeleton that has a one-to-one mapping with five-finger dextrous robot hands, and we call it DexUMI. Let's look at it in action. On the left, the human direct collecting the data is always the fastest. On the right, look at how difficult tele-op is, right? The human operator, here one of our most skilled PhDs, he has to align very carefully, right? And then it's super slow; also the success rate is very low as well. And in the middle, DexUMI, you just wear this exoskeleton and you collect data directly. And we train a robot policy on this data. So here what you see is a fully autonomous policy rollout that's trained on zero tele-operation data. So we're able to break the curse of 24 hours per robot per day and see how happy these robots are because they no longer need to be in the loop for data collection. So is this the answer? Have we solved scaling for robotics? Anyone driving Tesla or Waymo here, anyone? You know, when you're driving, you're actually contributing to the biggest physical data flywheel. And the beauty is you don't even feel it during FSD because the data upload is an ambient process. Yet wearing these UMI or data wearables, it's still cumbersome, right? It's intrusive. It's not as seamless as just driving to work. So we need an FSD equivalent. The data collection needs to get out of the way, fade into the background, so we can capture the full glory of human dexterity across all walks of life, across all labors of economic value. So we're going all in on human egocentric videos that come with these detailed annotations, like hand position tracking and dense language annotations. Introducing EgoScale, where 99.9% of the training that goes into this is based on human egocentric videos. And the result is an end-to-end policy that maps directly from the camera pixels here to 22 degrees of freedom high-dexterity robot hands. What you see here is fully autonomous. We pre-train EgoScale on 21k hours of in-the-wild egocentric human data with zero robot data whatsoever. During pre-training, we predict these hand joints and wrist poses. Then in action fine-tuning, we collect only 50 hours of glove data plus 4 hours of tele-op. That's 4 hours of tele-op, less than 0.1% of our training mix. And with this, EgoScale is able to generalize to these very dextrous tasks like sorting cards or syringe transfer, transferring the liquid. You know, someday we might have robot nurses at home, might as well try this. For these tasks, it takes only a one-shot robot demo at test time to learn the different shirt folding strategies. And perhaps the most fascinating finding from the paper is that we discovered this neural scaling law for dexterity. It's a very clean relationship between the amount of hours we put into pre-training and the optimal validation loss. In fact, it's a clean log-linear mathematical equation, six years after the original neural scaling law for language models. So if we put all of these data strategies on this chart—x-axis is alignment to hardware, y-axis is scalability—this is what it looks like. Tele-op, the least scalable. Data wearables, you can go up to hundreds of thousands of hours. And egocentric video, if we're able to spin the FSD flywheel, easily 10 million hours in the next year or so. And if we draw a line here, everything to the left of this line is the new paradigm: "sensorized human data." So let me make a few predictions. In the next year or two, we'll see tele-op dropping and dropping to an almost negligible amount. And then there will be an ensemble of data wearables custom-designed for different hardware and use cases. And finally, the main diet for robotics will be egocentric videos. So a moment of silence for our dear friend tele-op. You have served us well. Rest in peace. Long live sensorized human data. Are we done with the data strategy yet? Did you notice I put two rings on data strategy? What's the outer ring here? All the LLM frontier labs have spent significant budget now on acquiring millions of coding environments to do reinforcement learning. So robotics is the same. We're in urgent need to scale up environments. And of course, you can always do reinforcement learning directly on the real robot. So in our lab, we use RL to push certain tasks to almost 100% success rate, so you can do these continuous executions for hours on end. You know, it's kind of therapeutic to see these robots assembling GPUs just by themselves, or as a wise man would say, "Good boi." This task has been approved by my boss. Yet we can't get to 1 million environments because that would require 1 million robots if you do it the previous way. So we need a better way. Here, let's say you take an iPhone picture, and you can pass this through this 3D world scan pipeline to extract all the objects and then automatically synthesize them again inside a classical physics simulator. All these objects are actually interactive after the scan. And then you can augment this infinitely in simulation with variations that we call digital cousins. So now iPhone basically becomes a pocket world scanner in this process that we call real-to-sim-to-real. In this way, we have a scalable way to port the physical world into the digital world. But still, this method relies on a classical graphics engine. Can we do better? Introducing DreamDojo. So it's our spin on video world models and turning them into full-fledged neural simulators. DreamDojo takes as input these continuous action signals and outputs the next RGB frames as well as sensor states in real time. Not a single pixel you see here is real. And DreamDojo is able to capture and learn the mechanics of different robots through a purely data-driven approach. There's no physics equation, no graphics engine involved in this process. So the new post-training paradigm for robotics is a massively parallel RL system that runs on a few real robot stations, a bunch of graphics cores running world scans, and heavy inference compute running world models. Or as this equation goes: compute equals environment equals data. Or as a wise man would say, "The more you buy, the more you save." And this message has been approved by my boss. So that's it. Putting it together, "The Great Parallel" that robotics will follow. It's happening as we speak. And we're looking at the beginning of the endgame. You guys play the video game Civilization? Still my favorite. I like to think of my research as unlocking game achievements on this civilizational technology tree. And there are three more achievements to unlock for robotics, and then we're done. I can retire, and I can't wait for that. The first is passing the physical Turing test. Across a wide range of activities, you cannot tell the difference between a human doing the task or a robot doing it. Maybe not drunk humans, but you know, the physical Turing test is about energy in yields unit labor out. And just by judging at the sexy pose of this robot, I think the work is cut out for us. So maybe it's 2 to 3 years away. And next, physical API. You have a whole fleet of robots and they can be configured just like any other software using APIs and command lines, orchestrated someday by o9.0. And if we have this physical API, we'll be able to realize lights-out factories. Those are essentially printers of atoms. They take as input design in markdown files and then output fully assembled products, completely autonomous. Or these wet labs that automate scientific discoveries in chemistry, biology, and medicine. Finally, physical AutoResearch, when the robots start to design, improve, and build the next iteration of themselves far beyond what's humanly possible. Energy in yields next robots out. So you might ask, is this too science fiction? Like, are we going to see this in our lifetime? Well, it took the AI community 14 years to go from the first forward pass of AlexNet in 2012, a model that barely recognized cat versus dog, to AI Ascent today in 2026, where we talk about agentic AutoResearch. And let's just add another 14 years, how about that? 2026 is right in the middle of 2012 and 2040. And technology does not advance linearly, it advances exponentially. So I can say with 95% certainty that we'll get to the end of the endgame, the end of the technology tree by 2040. And we'll still be all—we'll still be young. "If you believe in robotics, robotics will believe in you." And to all of us here sitting here, I think our generation was born too late to explore the earth and too early to explore the stars, but we're born just in time to solve robotics.

Full Trump press conference discusses Iran war, World Cup, attempted shootings and more

USA TODAY(7.5M) ·59:28 ·3.5K views · 2026-04-30
Donald Trump (President of the United States)

TL;DR President Trump signed a historic executive order establishing "Trump IRAs" to expand retirement savings access with federal matching for low-income Americans, while simultaneously announcing an overhaul of federal contracting and providing updates on the "military...

TL;DR

President Trump signed a historic executive order establishing "Trump IRAs" to expand retirement savings access with federal matching for low-income Americans, while simultaneously announcing an overhaul of federal contracting and providing updates on the "military decimation" of Iran’s nuclear and defense capabilities.

Retirement Savings and Financial Initiatives

  • TrumpIRA.gov Launch: Starting next year, Americans can open low-cost retirement accounts modeled after the federal Thrift Savings Plan.
      • Federal Saver's Match: Low-income earners (under $35,000) are eligible for up to $1,000 per year in direct federal matching funds.
      • Long-term Wealth: Trump estimated that a 25-year-old investing $165/month with the match could accumulate $465,000 by age 65.
  • Phase Two Expansion: Economic Advisor Kevin Hassett and Chairman Jason Smith are working on legislation to expand these matches to middle-income Americans and "Uber drivers" who lack employer-sponsored plans.
  • Trump Accounts Success: Trump reported 5 million signups for related financial products, noting that Michael Dell contributed $6.25 billion to support the initiative.

Economic Policy and Federal Efficiency

  • Federal Contracting Reform: An executive order established "fixed-price" contracts as the default for the federal government, replacing "cost-plus" models to drive down fraud, abuse, and cost overruns.
  • Energy Dominance: Trump signed a presidential permit for the Bridger Pipeline, a transborder oil and gas pipeline described as a successor to the Keystone XL project.
  • Economic Indicators: The President highlighted 2% GDP growth, a $9 trillion increase in stock market value since his election, and IRS data showing average tax returns are up 11% due to the "Big Beautiful Tax Bill."
  • Tax Policy: Reiteration of "no tax on tips," "no tax on overtime," and "no tax on Social Security" for seniors.

Foreign Policy and Military Operations

  • Iran Conflict: Trump described a highly successful 45-minute military operation involving B-2 bombers from Missouri that "obliterated" Iran’s nuclear capacity.
      • Military Impact: He claimed Iran’s navy is "underwater," drone factories are down 82%, and missile factories are down 90%.
      • Diplomatic Stance: Despite the military action, Trump stated Iran is "dying to make a deal" because their economy is crashing under a total blockade.
  • Venezuela Partnership: Described a "joint venture" style relationship where Venezuela is producing more oil than it has in decades, benefiting both nations.
  • NATO Relations: Trump threatened troop withdrawals from Germany, Spain, and Italy, criticizing them for failing to assist in the Middle East and "making a mess" of Ukraine.

Personnel, Security, and Political Strategy

  • Surgeon General Shift: Formally withdrew Casey Means and nominated Dr. Nicole Saphier (formerly of Fox News and Memorial Sloan Kettering) as the new Surgeon General.
  • Assassination Attempt and Security:
      • Trump praised the Secret Service's performance during a recent attempt on his life but noted he is considering wearing a bulletproof vest, despite concerns about looking "20 pounds heavier."
      • He criticized Democrats for wanting to "defund" law enforcement, including ICE and the Secret Service.
  • Legislative Strategy: Trump called on Senate Republicans to "blow up the filibuster" immediately to pass the Save America Act and voter ID laws, warning that Democrats will do so the moment they regain power to add 21 Supreme Court justices and grant statehood to D.C. and Puerto Rico.

Notable Quotes

"If we terminated the filibuster, we would have a 50-year run. You’d stop cheating... they’re professional cheats and they’re great at it. That’s what they’re best at because you can’t get elected with their policy." — President Donald Trump
"Iran killed 42,000 people over the last two months, protesters... they are in very bad shape. They’ve got nothing going, including leadership. Their first-string leaders are gone. Their second-string leaders are gone. Now we’re dealing with the third string." — President Donald Trump

Hassett on Powell Staying at Fed, Inflation and Oil Spike

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·12:32 ·4.7K views · 2026-04-30
Kevin Hassett (Director, National Economic Council)
NEC Director Kevin Hassett anticipates a "sea change" at the Federal Reserve under Kevin Warsh’s expected leadership, advocating for interest rate cuts to fuel a productivity-led growth cycle while dismissing current energy spikes as temporary disruptions that do not warrant rate hikes.
TL;DR NEC Director Kevin Hassett anticipates a "sea change" at the Federal Reserve under Kevin Warsh’s expected leadership, advocating for interest rate cuts to fuel a productivity-led growth cycle while dismissing current energy spikes as temporary disruptions that do not...

TL;DR

NEC Director Kevin Hassett anticipates a "sea change" at the Federal Reserve under Kevin Warsh’s expected leadership, advocating for interest rate cuts to fuel a productivity-led growth cycle while dismissing current energy spikes as temporary disruptions that do not warrant rate hikes.

Federal Reserve Leadership and Independence

  • Leadership Transition: Hassett expressed disappointment in Chairman Jerome Powell’s recent comments but signaled that the administration is focused on the expected confirmation of Kevin Warsh as the next Chair.
      • "Sea Change": Warsh is viewed as "fresh leadership" that will restore institutional independence and shift the Fed's current trajectory.
      • Confirmation Outlook: Hassett described Warsh’s confirmation as a "lock."
  • Fed Independence: The administration maintains it respects the Fed’s autonomy despite public criticisms from the President.
      • Inspector General (IG) Role: Hassett defended the Justice Department’s request for the Fed’s IG to review internal matters, arguing it is a "completely respectful" use of the Fed’s own internal oversight.
      • De-escalation: The administration hopes for a de-escalation of tensions once the transition in leadership occurs.

Economic Outlook and Monetary Policy

  • "Blockbuster" Economic Data: Recent indicators show a robust economy, characterized by high capital spending and historically low unemployment metrics.
      • Capital Spending Boom: High import levels are being driven by capital goods used to build domestic factories, rather than perishable consumer goods.
      • Employment Strengths: Jobless claims are at their lowest since the 1960s, and government employment is at its lowest level since WWII.
  • The Case for Rate Cuts: Hassett argues that interest rates should be reduced to "supercharge" the economy, specifically targeting the housing market.
      • Residential Investment: Noted as a current weak spot (down 8%) due to interest rate sensitivity; lower rates would stimulate new construction and associated consumer spending.
      • Productivity Buffer: Like the 1990s "Greenspan era," Hassett believes a "productivity boom" will keep core prices under control despite high growth.
  • Inflation vs. Energy Shocks: Hassett warns that hiking rates in response to a "temporary spike" in energy prices would be a "policy error."
      • Core vs. Headline: The administration views core inflation as stable and a better predictor of future trends than the volatile topline inflation influenced by oil.

Energy Markets and the Iran Conflict

  • Strategic Pressure on Iran: High oil prices and $6/gallon gasoline in California are attributed to the geopolitical conflict with the Iranian regime and its nuclear ambitions.
      • Sanctions Impact: Hassett noted that Iranian production has previously been squeezed from 5 million to 1 million barrels per day under strict sanctions, and the administration intends to maintain pressure to stop their nuclear program.
      • Economic Blockade: Reports suggest Iran is facing hyperinflation, food shortages, and business closures as a result of the current blockade.
  • Domestic Mitigation Efforts: The White House has implemented several measures to shield Americans from price spikes:
      • California Drilling: Reopening offshore drilling in California that was previously shut down by the state.
      • Logistics & Standards: Implementing Jones Act waivers and adjusting ethanol standards to increase summer fuel supply.

International Relations and European Critique

  • Tensions with Germany: Hassett addressed recent friction between the President and the German Chancellor regarding Iran policy.
      • Policy Critique: Hassett characterized German energy and economic policies as "unwise" and "anti-free market," suggesting these policies have left Europe overly vulnerable to Middle Eastern energy shocks.
      • Growth Stagnation: He noted that European economies are currently struggling to achieve even 1% growth and urged partners to help resolve the Iranian nuclear threat to stabilize the global market.
"I think when Kevin [Warsh] gets in there, it’s going to really be a sea change at the Fed that we’re all looking forward to." — Kevin Hassett
"It would be a policy error for the ECB or the Fed to hike rates into a temporary spike in energy prices... The best forecast of future inflation is not topline inflation, but core inflation." — Kevin Hassett
"Our expectation is that things are going to get better very, very quickly if we can just get the Iranians to finish the negotiations and realize that it’s in the interest of their own people to stop things that could turn into serious hunger events." — Kevin Hassett

Stripe Sessions 2026 | Indexing the economy

Stripe(103.0K) ·30:15 ·5.1K views · 2026-04-30
The global economy is undergoing a structural "replatforming" around AI, characterized by a surge in high-revenue solopreneurship, "global-day-one" scaling for startups, and the emergence of autonomous agentic commerce supported by stablecoin infrastructure.
TL;DR The global economy is undergoing a structural "replatforming" around AI, characterized by a surge in high-revenue solopreneurship, "global-day-one" scaling for startups, and the emergence of autonomous agentic commerce supported by stablecoin infrastructure. SaaS...

TL;DR

The global economy is undergoing a structural "replatforming" around AI, characterized by a surge in high-revenue solopreneurship, "global-day-one" scaling for startups, and the emergence of autonomous agentic commerce supported by stablecoin infrastructure.

Current Economic Indicators and Myths

  • SaaS Resilience: Despite a $1 trillion market value loss in the software sector in January 2026, Stripe data shows SaaS payment volumes remain higher than pre-sell-off levels, indicating fundamental revenue strength.
  • Rational Markets: Outsize valuations have shifted strictly toward the most profitable companies, correcting the "dot-com" style irrationality where unprofitable firms were highly rewarded.
  • Tariff Pass-Through: While trade volumes remain stable, the downward price trend in durable goods (typically driven by manufacturing productivity) has reversed as businesses pass cumulative tariff costs to consumers.
  • The "K-Shaped" Myth: Contrary to popular narratives of a wealthy-only recovery, Stripe data shows the spending gap between high-income and low-income consumers is actually shrinking.
  • Labor Market Cooling: Recent unemployment upticks are attributed more to interest rates and "pandemic hiring hangovers" than to AI displacement.

The Explosion of Economic Dynamism

  • Solopreneurship at Scale: There is a massive re-acceleration in US business formations, driven primarily by "non-employer firms." Nearly 5 million Americans now earn their living as solopreneurs generating over $100,000 in revenue.
  • Accelerated Growth: The Stripe Atlas program recently hit 100,000 incorporations. The "Class of 2026" startups are tracking to five times the revenue of the 2025 cohort at the same stage.
  • Global-by-Default Playbook: Modern startups no longer wait to expand. The median top-100 AI startup on Stripe earns most of its revenue internationally and sells into 55 countries within its first year.
  • Coasian Shift: Based on Ronald Coase’s theory of the firm, AI is lowering the "coordination costs" between companies. This leads to a future of fewer people per firm, higher output per firm, and more coordination via efficient external markets.

The Rise of Agentic Commerce

  • Evolution of Autonomy: Commerce is moving from "Level 1" (software filling out forms) to "Level 2" (reasoning assistants like Wayfair’s shopping bot) toward "Level 5" (fully autonomous agents).
  • Machine-to-Machine Payments: Autonomous agents are already executing micro-transactions. John Collison demonstrated an AI agent researching energy markets and autonomously purchasing four cents worth of proprietary data to complete its task.
  • Infrastructure Requirements: To move agentic commerce into the mainstream, three layers are required:
      • The Economic Layer: Agents must be able to pay and businesses must accept those payments.
      • Micropayment Viability: For tiny, frequent transactions, stablecoins are becoming the essential infrastructure due to near-zero transaction costs.
      • The Trust Layer: Systems to manage permissions and verify agentic identity.

Strategic Moats in an AI Economy

  • Complementary Assets: As intelligence becomes cheap and abundant, its "complements" become more valuable. Key high-value assets now include:
      • Energy and Hardware: Massive demand for data center power is driving value for gas turbine manufacturers (e.g., Siemens Energy).
      • Proprietary Data: Companies like Reddit and Stripe (Radar) are monetizing data that was previously a "dormant asset" on the balance sheet.
      • Real-World Execution: Traditional firms like John Deere are maintaining moats through "complex interactions" between software and physical hardware (tractors in 130 countries).
  • The Productivity Lag (Solo Paradox): Historically, transformative technologies like electricity (1882) and computing (1987) took decades to show up in productivity statistics because the economy had to "digest" and redesign itself. Collison predicts the AI "digestive" period will be significantly shorter than the 30 years required for electrification.
"The markets are being tediously rational. The most profitable companies are the ones being rewarded with outsize valuations." — John Collison
"It used to be that young companies sold mostly at home, and only ventured out to the open sea of global commerce once they'd gotten big. Now, it's the newborns on Stripe who are globe-trotting their way through the long tail of markets." — Emily Sands
"Transformative technology looks for a long time like it's not doing much if you're looking at the economic gauges... but these are the early indicators of an economy that's replatforming itself." — John Collison

It's not revenue softness that's happening right now. SaaS payment volumes on Stripe are actually a good chunk higher today than before the sell-off. So SaaS is still growing just fine. What does seem to have come back into fashion, though, is profitability, and not just in software but across the entire equity market. This is a chart showing how much market cap has historically accrued to the most profitable and the least profitable companies in the index. You see in the middle here, this is the dot-com boom, where the markets went a bit mad for a moment and briefly the least profitable companies were worth more than the most profitable ones. But if you go forward to today, now, despite all the talk about bubbles, it's the opposite. The markets are being tediously rational. The most profitable companies are the ones being rewarded with outsize valuations. We heard a lot about trade policy over the past year. Believe it or not, it's only been a year since Liberation Day. It feels like eight years, but it's only been one year. And the common wisdom by now is that the tariffs were, kind of economically speaking, a nothingburger; you know, the dog that didn't bark. And you can make that case. Trade flows ended the year only a little bit below 2024 levels. But there were higher prices to absorb as a result of tariffs. And what happened is, early on you had businesses eating the higher costs. But now, quarter by quarter, they're starting to pass them through. We can throw up durable goods prices here. This is how they behave intra-year in normal years. You might be wondering why there is this downward trend. It's because you have deflation in durable goods. Manufacturing productivity generally increases, global trade adds competition, and your flat-screen TV gets cheaper or your washing machine gets better for the same price. So you usually, in most years, get this gentle, predictable slope downward. And you see this only goes up to '24. If we add '25, it’s totally different and bucks the trend. And if we add '26, it's only three months in, but the same thing is happening and even more so. Our view is that tariff costs are still working their way through to the consumer. The story is not written yet. Another phenomenon you might have heard about is this K-shaped economy, the idea that wealthy consumers are holding things up and they're an increasingly large share of the spending. Maybe you saw this go viral on Twitter. This is the new United 787-9 seat map. And you can see that most of the cabin is now dedicated to business class seats. Or you can look at Delta's ticket revenue. They reported that premium seat revenue is now bigger than economy for the first time. This stuff gets a lot of retweets, but we don't actually see it in Stripe's data. If we look at the ratio of high-income to low-income consumer spending in our data, you'd expect this chart to be going up. But it's the opposite. The line is trending gradually downwards. The gap between high-income and low-income spending has been shrinking. So we don't actually see, when we look for it, this overreliance of the economy on high-income consumers that everyone has been talking about. The other major economic topic is the worry that AI is taking all the jobs. And if you look at the numbers, it's true that the labor market is cooling slightly. US unemployment rates are ticking up about a percentage point over the past three years. But you can ask the question: how much of that is actually AI? Part of it is the delayed pandemic hangover. If you look at the hiring rate, companies really binged on hiring in '21 and '22, and they're still unwinding that. Tighter immigration played a bit of a role. And then, of course, you have interest rates. The Fed's latest forecast for the end of 2026 is half a point higher than they predicted at this time two years ago. So money is tighter, and tight money slows hiring. So AI is in the mix, and it's presumably going to have a big future impact, but it's one force among many and probably not the dominant one in the labor market numbers you've seen today. Now, you're probably wondering what we're seeing at Stripe and how it corresponds to what's going on in the wider world. Stripe now processes almost 2% of global GDP, so we have a really useful window into the forefront of the internet economy. There are three areas that I want to dive into deeper with you. The first is that we're seeing a structural increase in economic dynamism. More firms are getting started, they're keeping headcount lean for longer, but they're scaling revenue faster than we've ever seen before. There's plenty of talk about AI not showing up in the macro data yet, like the labor stats we just saw. If you're as AI-pilled as most of us in this room, that probably creates some cognitive dissonance. But here's the thing: AI actually is showing up in the macro data. You just have to know where to look. Here's US business formations over the past 20 years. You can see a huge bump during the pandemic, which is not surprising. But you can also see it re-accelerating now. Basically, all of the recent growth is coming from what the Census Bureau calls non-employer firms, or what we all call solopreneurs. That's the blue line here. Now, these didn't used to be considered serious businesses. But AI means more of these businesses are getting to real scale. This is the number of solopreneurs in the US doing over $100,000 in revenue. Yes, solopreneurship is now how close to 5 million Americans earn their living. And we're fortunate to have many thriving solopreneurs building with Stripe. It's a great crew. This isn't just an American story. New business registrations are up 40% in Australia, up 70% in Finland, and up 80% in France. So the surge in dynamism is happening across advanced economies. At Stripe, we're seeing this firsthand. So in aggregate, Atlas companies incorporated in 2025 are raking in twice as much revenue as the class of '24 was by this point. Nothing shabby about a doubling. But it's the class of 2026 that's really cooking. So let's zoom in a little bit and you can see that just a few months into the year, the class of '26 is tracking to five times the revenue of last year's cohort. These are very unusual growth rates. And they're driven in part by a generational shift in how companies grow internationally. So one simple way to see this is to ask across Stripe how many companies are earning most of their revenue outside their home country. Five years ago, that was 11.6%. Since then, it's doubled. And they aren't just selling in the obvious places. Among companies making most of their money cross-border, around a quarter are making most outside the top 10 global markets. It's a real long-tail story. The international pecking order has also flipped. It used to be that young companies sold mostly at home and only ventured out to the open sea of global commerce once they'd gotten big. Now, it's the newborns on Stripe who are globe-trotting their way through the long tail of markets. Take the top 100 AI startups on Stripe. The median earns most of its revenue internationally and sells into 55 countries within its first year of existence. Emergent Labs was founded in the US in 2024, but already nearly 70% of their revenue comes from abroad. They're doing material business in many markets. No less than 16 countries drive at least 1% of Emergent's revenue. So this is a totally new startup playbook. You launch globally on day one, you keep headcount very lean, and you automate aggressively. But not every business can be a solopreneur or a top AI startup. So what does all of this mean for the median firm? Despite the fact that one of us has a PhD in economics and the other dropped out of college and now hosts a podcast in a pub, John is going to teach us a little bit about economic history. John Collison: I think you might be surprised to learn just how many economic theories have been devised over a pint, Dr. Sands. Okay, some economic history. Are we ready? In 1931, a 21-year-old British student took a road trip across depression-era America. And back then, as now, a bunch of young people were pretty into communism. Central planning had this real intellectual prestige at the time. A lot of smart people thought that top-down coordination might be the way to do things, much better than messy, chaotic markets. And the student noticed something interesting. Within a firm, resources aren't allocated by prices. There's no little market inside the company. Companies themselves are centrally planned. And so he wondered, if markets are so efficient, why do we keep building little islands of central planning inside markets? His name was Ronald Coase. And his answer was that firms exist because coordinating inside a company is often actually cheaper and easier than coordinating through markets. And that answer won the Nobel Prize 60 years later. They don't rush into decisions over there in Stockholm. I know what you're thinking. John's little economic story is heavy on story and light on economics. But here's where we're going. What's the Coasian reading of AI? In the near term, the within-firm effect is the most obvious. Companies have shared context and systems of record and aligned incentives. And all of that allows for easier coordination with AI. You're probably feeling this in all your own companies already. But in the medium term, external markets are likely to get even more efficient. Agents are already great at discovery. They make it trivially easy to integrate a new piece of software. They make contracting much more straightforward. And as we'll discuss in a minute, they can transact much more frequently and for smaller amounts than humans can. Together, all of this should bring coordination costs between firms down by quite a bit. Now, some of these changes will take a little while to play out, but some of them are already here. So, on net, with AI, we expect fewer people per firm, more output per firm, just more firms, and more coordination happening through market-like mechanisms. Coase would have liked that idea. He always thought firms were an inefficient setup. The more we can do via markets, the better. An indie hacker from another era. Yeah, thanks Emily. Okay, so that was the explosion in business dynamism that we're seeing. The second trend I want to talk about is how commerce itself is becoming agentic. At Stripe, we think about this in increasing levels of autonomy in the purchasing flow, from simple help all the way up to significant agency. But you might be wondering, where have we actually gotten to in 2026? Well, level one is already here. This is software schlepping through forms on your behalf. The in-app checkout experience we're doing with Meta is actually a good example of this. So maybe you find something, maybe in an ad, you express interest and the agent has your details already and it can complete the checkout for you. Super handy, really convenient, but not exactly science fiction. I mean, you mightn't even think of this as agentic even though strictly speaking the software is doing the purchasing for you. It is your agent. Level two is the shift from plain old keyword search that we've had for decades to a shopping assistant that can actually reason within constraints and find products accordingly. Think about when you do some shopping on ChatGPT. Imagine, totally hypothetically, you say, "I need a birthday gift for my brother. He's 38 and has kind of weirdly at this late age gotten very into calisthenics, but he already actually has a lot of calisthenics gear. What's a non-obvious gift that I could get for him for under $100?" Hypothetically. It's not obvious what keywords you would put in to get these results. Wayfair does something similar. You can describe a room or a style or a feeling and the agent rummages through the catalog for you. And you're still in control here; you're making the buying decision. You don't have some awful vase you didn't want showing up unexpected. But you have better ways to find what you're actually looking for. Most people say they already shop this way; I mean you've probably all already done this. And there, the demand for autonomous commerce is really palpable. This is the cumulative downloads of payment-related skills on ClawHub: 125,000 in 12 weeks. And this is despite the fact that OpenClaw is still pretty hard to use for regular folks. So the question isn't whether there's demand; there is. Agents need to be able to pay, businesses need to be able to accept payments from those agents, and the whole thing needs a trust layer. And Stripe is working hard on getting all of this deployed. Really interesting question, which is my third topic: in a world where intelligence can do all of that, and everyone has access to that kind of intelligence, what actually becomes more valuable? There's an old rule in economics: when something gets cheap, its complements get more valuable. So, when containerized shipping collapsed the cost of moving goods, ports that could handle large container ships became much more valuable. So what are the complements to AI? One, certainly, is energy. We're going to need a lot more energy for AI's power to power data centers. In the meantime, we're going to need a lot of gas turbines, which you can read about in the report. You can see the order volumes taking off here. It's also reflected in the market value of the handful of companies that make them. This is Siemens Energy, one of the big gas turbine makers. But a less discussed complement to AI is proprietary data, which gets much more valuable when you can let super intelligent agents reason over it. One way you can tell data's getting more valuable is that companies that used to give it away for free have stopped doing that. This chart shows the percentage of various parts of the internet that have been shut off to AI crawling. And instead, those companies are starting to monetize it. Reddit has always had a ton of data; you know all the comments in the subreddits were always there. But before AI, it was a dormant asset on the balance sheet, and today their non-ad revenue, which comes from data agreements, is $35 million a quarter. We see the same dynamic with our own data; take Stripe Radar. Stripe Radar has always been able to reason across Stripe's entire corpus of data, but in recent years as the AI models have gotten better, the underlying data is then more valuable. Network effect is another one we should talk about. Buyers and sellers still need places to meet, and if switching costs are low, the network effects are even more important. To understand this better, we took a look at the public take rate for 10 top marketplaces. What you see is take rates flat for a few years and then you get this bump up during the pandemic, and then a steady increase over the past three years as marketplaces see higher returns to better AI techniques. The last complement I want to call out is just companies that have figured out the complex interactions between software systems and real-world execution. I think we'll see that that is an enduring moat. Take John Deere. If I asked you to name an AI beneficiary, John Deere might not be the first name that you'd think of. But they've spent years integrating GPS guidance and machine vision and sensor arrays into their equipment. And the defensible part, the moat here, it's not the AI. Lots of people could build the AI. It's having the tractors in the fields across 130 countries. So those are five things we think probably increase in value alongside AI, and therefore create even more durable competitive advantages in the years ahead. For all of you, AI should change how you think about your own competitive advantages. You might previously have built the best software in your space, but you might be finding that software is not the name of the game anymore. What you do have is powerful proprietary data, interesting network effects, real-world operations, and tools that took a decade to get right. All sorts of advantages that hold their value or even become more valuable in a world of abundant intelligence. So as we wrap up, will you indulge me in just a little more economic history? I can't—I can't resist. In 1882, Thomas Edison lit up 82 customers in Lower Manhattan using six dynamos. Finally, electricity in Manhattan! And for decades after, even as electricity adoption grew, productivity growth barely budged or even slowed down as the railroad investment boom started to wear off. And the economists were confused initially. I mean, we had this awesome technology in electricity; why wasn't it showing up in the statistics? And the problem wasn't the technology; the electricity did work. It was the economy that had to digest it. You see, factories had been built around steam: the shafts and the belts and the floor plans. It was all wrong for electricity. And it wasn't until we redesigned factories from scratch that the productivity gains finally appeared. People sometimes forget that this took a full 30 years from 1882 all the way to the late 1910s. And then in the 1910s, in that single decade, the growth rate of output per worker more than doubled. But there was this big lag. We saw the same phenomenon again with the birth of computing. In fact, economists have since dubbed this whole phenomenon the Solo Paradox, after Robert Solo's 1987 quip that computers were everywhere to be seen except in the productivity statistics. And he was right. They weren't to be seen, and they wouldn't be until the mid-1990s. Transformative technology looks for a long time like it's not doing much if you're looking at the economic gauges. You're kind of sitting there and you're tapping the gauge: is this thing on? I think this is what we're actually watching in real time with AI. You see the seeds of this in the phenomena I already mentioned. The minimum efficient size of a serious business is collapsing. Solopreneurs are scaling to seven figures and beyond. Agents are buying from agents. Companies are launching globally from day one. But none of that fits in the old model. These changes might yield productivity dividends tomorrow because we have to digest them. But they're the early indicators of an economy that's replatforming itself. The businesses you're all building now, they're not a footnote to AI history; they are the AI history. They are the story of the economic and productivity gains. Electrification took 30 years to reorganize the economy, but I suspect we won't need to wait anywhere near as long as that for AI. Thank you so much for being here. I hope you're getting a ton out of Sessions. Enjoy the rest of your morning, and I will see you back here for our final fireside with Daniel Gross and Nat Friedman this afternoon. Thank you. Gracias. Cohere's Aidan Gomez on AI's pricing divide, plus Big Tech earnings — 4/30/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·40:35 ·3.9K views · 2026-04-30
Aidan Gomez (Founder and CEO, Cohere) Daniel Newman (The Futurum Group)
Big Tech is committing a staggering $700 billion in capital expenditures to sustain booming AI cloud growth, but this spending is cratering free cash flow and facing a new "pricing divide" as high-performing, low-cost Chinese models begin to dominate global traffic.
TL;DR Big Tech is committing a staggering $700 billion in capital expenditures to sustain booming AI cloud growth, but this spending is cratering free cash flow and facing a new "pricing divide" as high-performing, low-cost Chinese models begin to dominate global traffic....

TL;DR

Big Tech is committing a staggering $700 billion in capital expenditures to sustain booming AI cloud growth, but this spending is cratering free cash flow and facing a new "pricing divide" as high-performing, low-cost Chinese models begin to dominate global traffic.

The Capex Crisis vs. Cloud Boom

Despite "blowout" earnings, the market is punishing several Big Tech firms as the cost of AI infrastructure begins to erode balance sheets.

  • Surging Revenue: The "bull case" remains supported by massive growth in cloud divisions.
      • Google Cloud: Grew 63% year-over-year.
      • AWS: Reported its best quarter in over three years.
      • Microsoft Azure: AI business revenue doubled.
  • Collapsing Free Cash Flow: The $700 billion combined CAPEX commitment from the "Big Four" is hitting margins hard.
      • Amazon: Free cash flow fell 95% in one year.
      • Meta: JPMorgan downgraded the stock, projecting negative free cash flow of $4 billion this year and $24 billion next year.
      • Microsoft: Gross margins hit their lowest level since 2022 due to data center depreciation.

The Global AI Pricing Divide

A significant shift in model economics is occurring as Chinese competitors release "frontier-level" models at a fraction of the cost of American alternatives.

  • The DeepSeek Threat: The release of DeepSeek V4 has demonstrated that Chinese models are now nearly as capable as OpenAI or Anthropic in coding and agentic work but are significantly cheaper.
  • Market Shift: On OpenRouter (a major AI traffic aggregator), Chinese and open-source models now account for the majority of traffic, with three of the top five models being Chinese.
  • The Premium Question: Fortune 500 buyers are increasingly questioning if the "Western premium" for frontier models is justifiable as capabilities between global providers converge.

Enterprise Strategy: Security and Efficiency

Aidan Gomez, CEO of Cohere, argues that the enterprise market is bifurcating based on trust and compute constraints rather than just raw price.

  • The Trust Barrier: For regulated industries (finance, government, energy), Chinese models are "non-starters" due to national security concerns and potential "backdoor" vulnerabilities.
  • Privacy & "Air-Gapped" Deployment: Cohere is winning by allowing models to run on-premise or in highly secure environments (e.g., "in a submarine with no internet") where data never leaves the customer's control.
  • Right-Sizing Models: While frontier labs build 10-trillion parameter models, Cohere focuses on efficiency.
      • Compute Constraints: Many enterprises only have access to 2–4 GPUs; they cannot run massive frontier models.
      • Cost Optimization: As companies move from pilot programs to production, CFOs are forcing a shift toward smaller, more efficient models to control token bills.

The Market Outlook: "Existential" Spending

Daniel Newman of The Futurum Group defends the massive Capex spend as a necessary survival tactic for the "hyperscalers" (Google, Microsoft, Amazon).

  • Risk of Under-spending: Newman argues the risk of falling behind in the GPU race is greater than the risk of over-spending. AWS is cited as an example of a company that was slow to the "Nvidia era" and has spent eight quarters "digging itself out of a hole."
  • The "Model as a Moat" Fallacy: Newman suggests that having the best model is a shallow moat compared to owning the compute infrastructure. If a major lab like OpenAI were to fail, the demand would simply shift to other providers (Anthropic or Gemini) because the compute remains the primary bottleneck.
  • Apple’s Advantage: Despite being perceived as "late" to AI, Apple owns the "experience layer." All major AI models are ultimately consumed on the iPhone, giving Apple a dominant position in the distribution of AI services.

Notable Quotes

"If you are going to be replacing your entire software engineering team with models that are writing your code, you probably don't want that coming from China. Because it might be subtly introducing vulnerabilities that you're not going to catch."

— Aidan Gomez, CEO of Cohere

"There is a bigger margin of error for these guys to be wrong over-spending than there is for them to be wrong under-spending... right now the biggest risk in their business is not over-investing."

— Daniel Newman, CEO of The Futurum Group

"We focus on high-security settings... We can deploy into a submarine a kilometer under the surface of the ocean with no ability to talk to the internet. That level of security gives us a completely unique value proposition."

— Aidan Gomez, CEO of Cohere

"Folks like us... feel absolutely overwhelmed, bombarded and insulated by things AI. But the world is still very immature in terms of AI adoption. Only about 2% of the population is paying for AI right now."

— Daniel Newman, CEO of The Futurum Group

Hey everyone, Big Tech just posted blowout earnings, but Meta and Microsoft they are falling anyways as AI spending eats into their balance sheets. There's going to be another phase that we enter into where CFOs are looking at the expenses being spent on some of these models and they're going to try to optimize. They certainly aren't going to pull back and it's going to continue to grow, but we're going to need to find ways to use smaller models and more efficient models. I hope that doesn't mean shifting over to a Chinese tech stack to satisfy that. I think there are companies like Cohere and others who are building more aligned technology that satisfies that efficiency need. Right. But it does sort of beg the question, are the American models better than Chinese ones? Like what is the gap? Is DeepSeek V4 a real threat especially when we see sort of it matching or surpassing some of the frontier ones on the benchmarks? I think it's very close. The gap between the two countries, in addition to Canada, in addition to France, Germany, it's closing rapidly. Right. And you're saying that there's options. I mean Cohere's working on it, Nvidia's working on it, the startup Reflection is working on open source. Is there enough companies working on it in America and what happens to OpenAI and Anthropic if they aren't working on this sort of open-source model race? I think there's a lot of folks contributing to open source. At Cohere we've been investing in it for years now. I'm not so concerned about the number of players. I think there will continue to be good open-source options coming from democratic nations like the US, like Canada, like Germany. But I do think that the thing that China is doing that's setting it ahead is effectively distilling the large models. This is against the terms of service of a lot of these large models and so that gives them a shortcut. They can very quickly catch up just from distillation of someone else's work. Whereas for the rest of us that are trying to build a completely independent stack from scratch, it requires more effort and more work to build competitive great models. But I do see things converging. I do see capabilities converging between all the major providers. So what does that tell us about sort of the huge infrastructure spend that we see from the hyperscalers? Meta just talked about this coming off of massive earnings days. They're all planning to spend more than 700 billion dollars in infrastructure this year. From where you sit, especially as Cohere is building these very specialized models on just a few GPUs, does that money come back? Or do you think that the trend is going towards these smaller more specific models, the kind that Cohere's doing? I definitely think it's pointed towards more efficient models. There's going to be a big wave in the market seeking to reduce costs and make things more efficient. It's easy to do POCs on some of these large models, but then as soon as you push that into production and you're dealing with production-level scale, suddenly the price tag just changes the buying equation. And cost becomes a huge constraint. So I see things moving heavily in Cohere's direction. We've seen that over the past year with our revenue 6Xing last year and continuing to grow very rapidly this year. So I do think there's going to need to be a lot more infrastructure because the demand is virtually insatiable. But as that infrastructure comes online, we're also going to make a big push towards efficiency. So there will be much more AI in the world, but those AIs will be using less GPUs to do the same work as they were doing six months ago. Are the hyperscalers plus OpenAI and Anthropic, are they building for that kind of world? The hyperscalers are definitely building the infrastructure necessary to serve a significant amount of this demand. Although it's important to say that beyond cloud, on-prem deployments and private deployments, they're growing massively as well. And this is becoming an increasingly well-understood security threat that moving too much to the cloud is a mistake and exposes you to vulnerabilities especially in this new frontier of potential cyber attacks thanks to these models. And so on-prem deployments, folks building their own data centers, Neo-clouds, you're going to see a whole new array of compute providers to satisfy all of this demand. So certainly the hyperscalers are building out the infrastructure that is needed, but they won't capture the entire market. They'll probably capture half of it and the rest will be these on-prem security deployments. So where does that leave an OpenAI or Anthropic that's not, you know—the cloud business is not the main business, it's frontier models? I think the demand for frontier models and the demand for all models is going to be extraordinary. I think there's going to be—so it's basically a rising tide lifts all boats. Everything is going to benefit from this. I'm not too worried about the demand for their products or our own. There is a huge market out there. The applications for AI in the enterprise side, it feels like we're still scratching the surface. Like we're still doing very simple things. We know the models are capable; we know the models from 18 months ago are capable of way more than we're doing actually out in the industry. So even if you don't update the models for a year and a half, the industry is still catching up. So the demand piece, I feel extremely confident. There's a lot to go do in the global economy. A good question from Carson Allen, who's a regular viewer of our live stream, asking: are your pricing increasing as you see these compute constraints? No, so we don't actually charge our customers for the compute. We deploy inside of their private deployment. So it's their own compute. What we see with our customers purchasing their own compute, yes, the GPUs are becoming more expensive and there is a scarcity. You know, it's something that we try to help our customers with but our model of sales is not to provide the compute with the model; instead, we just provide the software. Like to automate workflows for big banks, you know, inside Capital Markets divisions, wealth management, investment banks, we automate work and augment employees inside those organizations and we don't build the actual data centers themselves or help source it. They do that and you come in and implement the tools, the AI tools. Got it. And so it's going to have very sweeping impacts and touch everyone's lives, both professionally and as consumers.
