Siemens & Adidas CEOs über Digitale Fertigung, KI und Innovation in der Industrie

Siemens(31.8K) ·93:44 ·42 views · 2026-04-30
Roland Busch (CEO, Siemens) Björn Gulden (CEO, Adidas)
The integration of industrial AI, digital twins, and 3D printing is dramatically accelerating manufacturing speeds and enabling mass-personalization, though German bureaucracy and high energy costs pose significant risks to European competitiveness a...
TL;DR The integration of industrial AI, digital twins, and 3D printing is dramatically accelerating manufacturing speeds and enabling mass-personalization, though German bureaucracy and high energy costs pose significant risks to European competitiveness against the rapid...

TL;DR

The integration of industrial AI, digital twins, and 3D printing is dramatically accelerating manufacturing speeds and enabling mass-personalization, though German bureaucracy and high energy costs pose significant risks to European competitiveness against the rapid scaling seen in China and the US.

Industrial AI & The Digital Twin Strategy

  • Digital Twin Efficiency: Siemens CEO Roland Busch highlighted that creating digital replicas of products and factories reduces development cycles from 40 months to 20 months or less.
      • Impact: This approach yields 20–30% (and up to 50%) time savings and significantly increases energy efficiency and variability.
  • AI Agents in Programming: Siemens has launched AI "engineering agents" that can autonomously write and validate control software.
      • Productivity Gains: This technology reduces manual workload by 50% and increases code quality by 80% compared to traditional human engineering.
  • Predictive Maintenance: In modern "smart" buildings, AI agents monitor occupancy and sensor health to optimize temperature and lighting, moving from scheduled maintenance to a "need-based" model.

The Future of Sports Performance & Manufacturing

  • Hyper-Personalization: Bjørn Gulden (Adidas) predicts that within 5 to 10 years, consumers will scan their feet in-store to receive 100% custom-produced shoes, moving away from standard sizing.
  • 3D Printing (Additive Manufacturing): Adidas is currently producing "4D" shoes that are fully automated and printed without handwork.
      • Technological Limits: While current 3D printing is slower and more expensive than mass production, it allows for innovations like the 97-gram marathon shoe that can improve professional run times by four minutes.
  • AI in Marketing: Adidas is already shifting toward "synthetic" (AI-generated) models for e-commerce photography to save time and cost, though major stars like Lionel Messi will remain "real."

Global Competition & The "China Factor"

  • Scaling Advantage: China leads globally in scaling new technologies. While a single machine might print a shoe in Germany, China deploys "football fields" of 3D printers to drive costs down.
  • Local-for-Local Strategy: Geopolitical pressures and shifting tariffs (especially in the US) are forcing companies toward decentralized production.
      • Market Differences: China’s consumers prioritize digital integration and "car-as-a-living-room" features, whereas European markets are fragmented across 26 different regulatory environments.
  • Battery Tech Dominance: Busch noted that China has effectively "decoupled" from the rest of the world in lithium-ion battery technology and supply chain ecosystems.

Workforce Transformation & Cultural Shifts

  • The Death of Coding: Roland Busch stated that "software is solved," meaning the role of the traditional coder is being replaced by "Architects" who manage AI agents to write code 100,000 times faster.
  • Skills Over Roles: Humans will not necessarily be replaced by AI, but by "humans who can operate AI." Adidas is focusing on "Prompting" as a vital new professional skill.
  • Leadership as a Marathon: Both CEOs emphasized physical fitness and team-sport experience as foundational for managing global corporations. Busch trains 6 days a week (primarily strength/circuits), while Gulden uses "Rollski" (roller skis) daily.
  • Education Investment: Siemens invests €430 million annually in employee training and education to keep the workforce "future-proof" amidst the rapid 7–8 year disruption cycle of AI.

Systemic Hurdles: Bureaucracy & Infrastructure

  • Bureaucracy Costs: Administrative hurdles cost German industry an estimated €13 billion annually.
      • Specific Examples: Busch criticized "Gold-Plating" (adding extra German regulations on top of EU laws) and cited cases where individual animal protection (e.g., a single lizard) stops major infrastructure or military projects.
  • The "Leisure Gap": Busch pointed out that Germans work an average of 400 hours less per year than the OECD average, raising concerns about the national "performance principle."
  • Infrastructure Deficit: Bjørn Gulden noted that Norway’s sports success is due to conscious investment in school-based facilities, whereas German public building costs are 2.5x higher than private sector projects due to over-regulation.
Notable Quotes

>

"The human is the most difficult factor. 80% of your thoughts as a CEO are always about people—how to motivate them and how to change them in the face of AI and crises." — Bjørn Gulden, Adidas CEO

>

"Most humans won't be replaced by AI, but by humans who know how to use AI. My advice to young people: Do not treat this as a hobby; use it productively." — Roland Busch, Siemens CEO

>

"Democracy in Germany has become bureaucracy. If I read every regulation and document required for a single Saturday event, you’d simply die. We must get honest about this if we want growth." — Bjørn Gulden, Adidas CEO

>

"The steam engine took 60 years to change the world; electricity took 30; the internet took 15. We are talking about 7 to 8 years for AI—and three are already gone." — Roland Busch, Siemens CEO
Was haben 3D-gedruckte Schuhe, künstliche Intelligenz und die Zukunft des Sports gemeinsam? Mehr als Sie denken! Willkommen zu Siemens Tech Minds 2026. Heute bei uns auf der Bühne: der CEO von Siemens, Roland Busch, und der CEO von Adidas, Bjørn Gulden. In dieser Panel-Diskussion treffen persönliche Perspektiven auf globale Trends. Wir sprechen über die Rolle des Sports im Leben, über moderne Produktion bis hin zu der Frage, wie moderne Technologien wie KI ganze Industrien verändern. Und welche Rolle spielt China, lokale Märkte und Investitionen in Sport-Infrastruktur? Jetzt reinschauen und erfahren, was die nächste Generation von Producern und Performern antreibt. Herzlich willkommen Roland Busch und Bjørn Gulden. Hallo! Servus Roland, grüß dich. Bjørn, grüß dich auch, hallo mein Lieber. So, bevor wir uns um die KI kümmern, starten wir mal ganz analog mit der sportlichen Basis. Roland, du leitest einen hochkomplexen Technologiekonzern, du bist CEO. Was würdest du sagen, ist die größte Schnittmenge zwischen einem CEO von Siemens und einem Leistungssportler? Also zunächst mal Fitness. Ich glaube, die körperliche Fitness gehört auch in meinem Beruf mit dazu. Wenn man einmal um die Welt fliegt innerhalb von zwei, drei Tagen und dann aussteigt, muss man Leistung abrufen, egal in welcher Zeitzone man ist. Da hilft körperliche Fitness einfach enorm. Episode 43: Jensen Huang on Generative Computing, Re-industrialization, & Physical AI

Special Competitive Studies Project(91.9K) ·42:36 ·14.5K views · 2026-04-30
Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA)
The shift from retrieval-based to generative computing marks a new industrial revolution that offers a historic opportunity for U.S.
TL;DR The shift from retrieval-based to generative computing marks a new industrial revolution that offers a historic opportunity for U.S. re-industrialization, provided the nation overcomes "existential" fears and prioritizes the rapid adoption of AI across all sectors of the...

TL;DR

The shift from retrieval-based to generative computing marks a new industrial revolution that offers a historic opportunity for U.S. re-industrialization, provided the nation overcomes "existential" fears and prioritizes the rapid adoption of AI across all sectors of the economy.

The Shift to Generative Computing

  • Paradigm Shift: Computing is moving from retrieval-based (accessing pre-recorded data like photos or stories) to generative (creating new information/tokens based on context and intent).
      • The Token Economy: AI reformulates numbers (tokens) into text, images, or biology/physics data. Huang likens this to "Tang"—the data is in powder form, and the computer adds "water" to create the final output.
      • Compute vs. Storage: This new era requires massive "AI Factories" (computers the size of football fields) rather than just centers of data storage.
  • NVIDIA’s Role: NVIDIA defines itself as a computing fabric provider, building the systems, software, and algorithms that connect chips to diverse industries like drug discovery (Lilly) and heavy equipment (Caterpillar).

The Five-Layer AI Cake & Re-industrialization

Huang identifies five critical layers where the U.S. must maintain leadership: Energy, Chips, Infrastructure, Models, and Adoption.

  • Economic Opportunity: AI is a market force capable of driving the re-industrialization of the United States, creating high-skilled manufacturing jobs that do not necessarily require a master’s or PhD.
      • Investment: NVIDIA has committed half a trillion dollars in consumption to move its supply chain from the East back to the West.
  • The Energy Bottleneck: Re-industrialization requires massive energy to "transform atoms."
      • Grid Modernization: The U.S. needs more efficient power grids with better service-level agreements to handle the energy demands of AI factories.
      • Sustainability: Sustainable sources like nuclear, solar, and fusion are essential for long-term growth.

Agentic and Physical AI

  • Agentic Systems: The current breakthrough is "harnessing" large language models to ground truth, research tools, and memory.
      • Automation: Most software tasks are now automated, though this is leading to *more* hiring of software engineers to handle increased complexity and innovation.
  • Physical AI: The science of robo-taxis is "solved" and now enters an engineering phase.
      • Humanoid Robots: Huang predicts these are 1–3 years away from becoming mainstream.
      • Challenges: The primary hurdles are mechatronics (motors, hands, weight-to-strength ratios) and battery technology rather than just the AI models themselves.

Geopolitics, China, and Open Source

  • Export Controls: Huang argues the U.S. should not concede the Chinese market, noting NVIDIA's market share in China has dropped from high levels to nearly zero due to current policies.
      • Dynamic Policy: He advocates for a "dynamic" export policy that favors the American tech stack while ensuring American companies lead globally in market share.
  • Open Source Security: Huang contends that open source enhances safety.
      • Defensive Swarms: Protecting against a "super agent" cyber attack requires "massive swarms" of defense agents built on open-source technology.
      • OpenShell: NVIDIA created "OpenShell" (a reference to "OpenCLAW" guardrails) to provide a virtual sandbox for agents, ensuring data privacy and policy control.

The Labor Paradox: Tasks vs. Purpose

Huang addresses the "stigma" and fear surrounding AI-driven job displacement.

  • Task vs. Purpose: AI automates tasks (like coding/typing), not jobs (like innovation/problem-solving).
      • The Radiologist Example: Ten years ago, experts predicted AI would eliminate radiologists. Today, there is a shortage of radiologists because AI enhanced the field rather than replacing the human professionals.
  • Software Engineering: While AI can write code, the demand for code is infinite. Huang argues we don't just need a "billion lines of code," we need a "trillion," and AI enables humans to reach that scale of ambition.
"The first layer [of the cake] is energy, then chips, infrastructure, models—and the most important part for every country is the adoption of the technology. This is the layer I'm most concerned about. We cannot afford for another country to leave us behind." — Jensen Huang
"Being human means to innovate and solve problems. Coding is just a task, like typing. If we didn’t have to type anymore, we could go and do all the things we imagine in healthcare, science, and manufacturing." — Jensen Huang
"We are so cinematic, so science fiction in the way we describe AI that we're causing consternation and fear. Meanwhile, the rest of Asia is embracing AI with great enthusiasm. This is how we get left behind." — Jensen Huang
Hi, I'm Ylli from SCSP.ai. For my next episode of Memos to the President, I just hosted Jensen Huang for the second conversation in less than 12 months here in our office space in Crystal City. We talked about layers of AI, we talked about China and export controls, and we talked about AI adoption and open source. Take a look at it; it's an incredible conversation ahead of our biggest event, AI+Expo, which takes place next week, May 7th through 9th, at D.C.'s Convention Center. If you haven't registered yet, please do so. We expect 20,000 people and incredible programming. Register, but first, watch the episode of my conversation with the CEO of Nvidia, Jensen Huang. Welcome back. I'm here sitting down for the second time in less than a year with the founder and CEO of Nvidia, Jensen Huang. Welcome. "Very happy to be here." It's an honor to have you, Jensen. And, you know, it's incredible. I was watching our podcast from last July. So many things have happened. So many things have evolved in the AI space. But let's start by talking about the state of AI first. You talk about the AI scale versus computer scale and what that means today and how it's different from the computer age. Can you elaborate on that a little bit? We're in a new computing paradigm. Artificial intelligence, if you break it down to the basics of computing, is essentially retrieval-based computing. The way we used to do computing is essentially retrieval-based computing. We would pre-record information—take a picture, record a video, write a story, do a brief, and so on—and we put it online. That's why we would put it in a data center, a center of data. And then based on whatever retrieval method you use, if you're shopping, it's probably a recommender system; if you're doing search, it recommended a list and you point and click; or if you use YouTube, it just feeds you whatever it feeds you. And so, then once you select, it would go and retrieve that file for you. And if you were a shopper, then it's very likely that based on your previous preferences and whatever context it is—where you are and from what other link did you come from, right, all the cookies you left behind—it might use that information to recommend one of maybe 16 versions of ads that's already created for you. It's all retrieval-based computing. So far, all of the way we largely use computing, we create content and we retrieve content. Well, the way that we do computing now with artificial intelligence is based on your context, based on your request, based on your intentions. It would then generate from some initial seed of information—it could be grounded in some fact, it could be grounded in a piece of report—and it generates information for you for the very first time. And every single time it's, you know, largely different. And so it's generative computing. It used to be retrieval-based, now it's generative. The benefit, of course, is that every time you use computing based on your context, based on the changes of ground truth, and based on your intention, intelligence would be applied and provide you the information that best suits you. And so that—makes sense? Okay, that's the big breakthrough. And the reason for that is we need so much more computation instead of a bunch of storage; we need a lot of computers. That's the fundamental difference. Now of course, the big breakthrough is because of that, we have to learn how to perceive information of all kinds—it could be text, it could be video, it could be images—we have to reason about what it is that you're asking me, the context and your intent, and then I have to plan to answer your question accordingly. It could be providing you an answer, providing you a summary, or maybe writing you a brief based on all the things that I've researched, or even doing some shopping for you. And so, we went from retrieval-based computing to generative, and on top of that, we've now made it possible for us to do tasks. And so, artificial intelligence is really about that journey. Right. I want to ask you a personal question here. When was the first time you saw a chatbot and how did you react to that? The first time I saw a chatbot was shortly after Nvidia built Megatron. Before the first large language model was announced, Nvidia announced with Microsoft the first large language model. It was called Megatron. And Megatron was about 400, 500 billion parameters. The technology that we invented to make it possible is pre-training the models. And together between us and Microsoft, we pre-trained a really large model at the time. This is probably late 90—mid-91, late 91. Okay, so this was before ChatGPT. And the thing that was great about it, of course, was that it encoded and was able to memorize a lot of knowledge. But the thing that we didn't invent that OpenAI invented to make ChatGPT useful is the concept of alignment, reinforcement learning, and human feedback. And as a result, you know, you give it a prompt, the Megatron—you give Megatron a prompt, it would generate just a—it would start spewing off all kinds of things that it was encoded in its memory, and it was just nonsense. And it was really—it wasn't very useful. And it was—we were waiting for another great invention; it was reinforcement learning and human feedback. But the first time I saw ChatGPT, I was mind-blown. It was—yeah, I was so happy to see it. Yeah. We have a segment we do every week here in our office called AI in the Office. We record every week one of our staffers, what did they do with AI last week? What did you do with AI last week that really was interesting? Yesterday, I was using it to finish my shareholder letter. And so I'll outline something and then I'll say, "Go off and read everything that I've already read, I've already said, and all the things that I've already written—all the keynotes that I've already done—and based on this outline, just populate it with things that I've already said. Just give me a basic framework." And then from that, I might take it and refine that, or I'm like, "Oh, that wasn't very good, I'll rewrite it all together." And then I would give it to the AI again to ask it to make it tone-consistent or, you know, highlight some things that it could do a better job on. Going back to Nvidia, writing is so hard—I hate writing—but you know, with AI it was tolerable. Yeah. Nvidia is more than just a chips company. It builds an entire ecosystem. And when you look at what you do, you not only invest in building the ecosystem that got you here, but you also build an ecosystem going forward. So how would you describe in Washington, what does Nvidia do? What is that ecosystem that you have built? We build the computing infrastructure for modern AI. And so, if describing modern AI as the new computing paradigm, then we are the computing infrastructure, the computing fabric that makes that possible. A computer consists of the chips but also the systems, the system software, and the algorithms, where it's computing—it could be in a data center, it could be on-prem, it could be inside a factory, or it could be in a base station like the work that we do with telecommunications. It could be, you know, in a car. And so, it doesn't matter really where computing is done, but we create the computing infrastructure necessary to do that. Each one of these different applications also has different algorithms, and our job is to translate the intention of the science to best fit into our architecture. And so Nvidia is really this collection of software, middleware, and essentially the operating systems of these domains that makes it possible for people to do all this computing. And then we connect all of that to the world's ecosystem of partners, whether it's Lilly for drug discovery or Caterpillar in energy generation, construction, and heavy equipment. And the number of companies we work with in the United States is basically everybody. Yeah. You have talked recently about AI being a five-layered cake. So I want to get into each one of the layers, and I want you to tell us—we're in Washington—where do we stand in each one of the layers? What are the things we need to do? What are the things where we are ahead? AI is a new computing paradigm. It is also a new industry altogether. And the reason for that is because the way that AI operates is so fundamentally different than the way computers operate. It no longer stores the information exactly as you described it and retrieves it from storage, from a disk drive or SSD. We now generate it, and the generation output is numbers. And those numbers are essentially tokens; we call them tokens. No different than—well, tokens are just a floating point number or some number. And we take those numbers and we reformulate them into the output that you desire. And the output you desire could be text, it could be video, it could be images, it could be sound. You know, kind of like Tang—it's in powder form and we add water to it and it turns into orange juice. And so we reformulate it in such a way that you can use. Well, that token generation process requires really large computers. And computers need energy. And so, the way that this industry has formulated is one: energy on the bottom; a whole bunch of chips and systems, computers—and these computers are fairly gigantic. I mean, you know, they're the size of football fields. And there's a lot of them. And so the next layer up from that is what we call infrastructure. Infrastructure is land, power, and shell, but also the software for cloud services. And then on top of that is models. And on top of that is really the most important part for the United States and every single country: the adoption of the technology. And this is in fact the layer that I'm most concerned about. There's one area that really is worthy of us spending time to talk about: making sure that we are mindful about, on the one hand, making sure we have proper guardrails and keep people safe in the application of the technology, but ensuring that the United States is in the front, at the pioneering front of applying the technology. It drives so much productivity and prosperity and technology as well as economic leadership. We can't afford for another country to leave us behind. We were the frontrunners of applying technology in the last industrial revolution. We need to be careful not to be the last in this industrial revolution. And so the five-layer cake starts with energy, chips, infrastructure, models. And even models, we of course think about large language models, which is the thing that we can engage with easiest and most. But remember AI can represent information of any kind, and some of the most important information we represent isn't language and numbers but biology, chemicals, physics, articulation, and animatronics. All of these types of information are represented by these tokens. And it's really important to recognize that when we think about AI, don't just think about the chatbot and the services we're talking about; there's enormous industries that are adjacent to it that don't get the voice that some of the AI companies get, but they're super important to the future of our nation. And so the AI models and the applications are on top. Yeah. Can we talk a little bit about the energy? Obviously all these models require a lot of energy going forward. We have a lot of hurdles in that space from the grid to building the necessary pathways. We work a lot on fusion, but you know, solar—so there's a variety of ways to get to the energy needed. How would you assess our energy situation right now? Well Ylli, that's a really important question. I think first of all, you've gotta take a step back and ask ourselves: do we want to re-industrialize the United States? Do we want to bring back to the United States this entire sector of the economy, this entire sector of labor that we consider essential to having a properly shaped society and a properly shaped economy? We've become a nation and an economy where unless you get a four-year degree, unless you get a master's or PhD, you're going to get left behind. And that's—that's unfortunate and unnecessary. I think we can all agree that that's unfortunate and unnecessary. We have to re-industrialize this country. For the first time in a generation, we have a market force that's incredibly powerful to drive the re-industrialization of our country. AI will cause us to create several plants. The first plant is chip plants. We're the largest AI company in the world today. We committed half a trillion dollars of consumption so that we can bring the supply chain from the East into the West—back to the West—so that we can build chip plants and packaging plants and computer plants so that we can build all of the manufacturing necessary for Nvidia's AI supercomputers to be built here and used here. So the first plants are chips, the second plants are associated with the computers themselves, and the third is putting these computers into AI factories. Altogether, we're talking about trillions of dollars of manufacturing and high-skilled labor jobs. We're going to create enormous amounts of manufacturing opportunity here in the United States. That, I think, is the first thing we have to confront: Do we want to be that country or not? Do we want to be that kind of society or not? Do we want to be that economy or not? Now, if we decide the answer to that is yes, then we obviously need energy because you need energy to transform atoms in one form to atoms in another form. You guys understand what I'm talking about. You have to break covalent bonds and you have to make covalent bonds. And in order to do that, an enormous amount of energy is necessary to change the phase of matter. And so, the whole concept of manufacturing is about energy. And so, we need energy in this country in order for us to re-industrialize. And that's—but as you build these—we are—and you ask me a question, I'm sorry—what is the state of our capabilities? Clearly, we are behind because of all of the policies we've had in the past. All the concerns about climate change caused us to, of course, under-invest in energy. Now, we want to be conscious about the environment. Everything has to be balanced. And so now the question is what do we do going forward? The first thing that we can do is we can modernize the grid; we can make the grid more efficient. We over-provision in the grid, as you know. We have to make sure that our grid can take care of our society and all of our infrastructure on 12 of the worst days of the year; the rest of the time it is over-provisioned by enormous amounts. And so the question is what kind of service level agreements can we come up with so that the power utilities can provide excess energy when they can? And during the times when they can't, now we have to provide for subsequent backup energy. The backup energy can come from solar, it can come from nuclear, you know, all kinds of sustainable energy in the future. But we have an opportunity right now with this incredible market-driven force to take advantage of this opportunity to, one, make sure that the United States becomes a manufacturing nation again and create enormous amounts of manufacturing labor and high-skilled, high-paying jobs. And then the second is to use this opportunity to enhance the energy system of our country. In July when we last spoke, you talked about the next wave of AI being agentic AI. My goodness. My goodness. Yeah. Can you talk a little bit about how you assess the state of agentic AI right now? There's a lot of conversation yesterday we've had here and in town about the state of agentic AI—you know, the successes, the failures, and what's happening right now. You know, the big breakthrough from large language models to chatbots was reinforcement learning and human feedback. The large breakthrough from large language models to agentic systems is a system called "harnesses." The model itself advanced, no doubt—the pre-training is improved, the reinforcement learning has improved, and teaching it how to reason better and tool use—all of that has improved. However, the big breakthrough is the concept of harnessing these large language models so that they have a connection to ground truth so that they could do research, so that they could use the web browser, so that they could reason and have memory and improve themselves and communicate with others. And so these agents, in the last six months, made enormous breakthroughs. Almost all of them perform incredibly well. We're big fans of Codex; Codex and Cloud Code both are incredibly good. Codex 5.5 just came out—huge breakthrough, big leap—and Cloud Code is obviously incredibly good. The thing that we can now do is that the vast majority of software tasks are now completely automated. And meaning that we don't have to do the programming ourselves. The one interesting observation, which was the prediction, was because of agents coming out, all software engineering jobs would be gone. That the first thing you should do is, whatever you do, plan on being a lot of things in the future but don't be a software engineer. Well, turns out the number of software engineers we're hiring is increasing. Every company is increasing; the number of software engineering jobs is increasing. And the reason for that is this—it's a very big idea. This also happened in radiology. Ten years ago it was somebody predicted that the first job that's going to go, the first industry that's going to go, is radiologists; that job is gone forever. And the reason for that is computer vision was going to completely transform studying these images. The prediction was 100% right: AI has now permeated every aspect of radiology. The only thing that was wrong was the prediction: radiologists are in short supply. And so the same thing is happening in software engineering. The reason for that is this: in our jobs, the task that we do—in the case of software engineering, the task is programming. You could argue it's kind of a fancy version of typing. However, the skill, the purpose of the job, is not programming; the purpose of the job is not coding. The purpose of the job is to innovate, solve problems, connect with collaborators, find problems that exist and solve them, or find problems that nobody's even expressed—that's called innovation—connecting unrelated things, creating something new. That's the purpose of software engineering. And so our engineers—their purpose in life is to innovate, solve the problems, and move the company forward. It includes coding, but coding is not their job; coding is their task that some of them do in their jobs. As I was saying, the next wave you predicted was the physical AI. Ten months later from where we last spoke about this topic, where do you think we are right now in the physical AI space? One of the best breakthroughs in physical AI—the first, the easiest one—is self-driving cars. Robo-taxis are here. The science is now completely solved. Now it's a lot of engineering. And even the engineering is just around the corner. So we're going to have robo-taxis. Have you ever used one of them by the way? "Oh yeah, yeah, sure, sure." How was your experience using it? Excellent. Nvidia makes robo-taxis. We created this software called AlphaM-IO; it's the world's first thinking robo-taxi, the thinking car, meaning it can come up to a circumstance that it has never seen before and it would reason about it. The way it reasons about it, it decomposes it into, you know, more mundane things. Oh, I've seen this, I've seen that, and in composition I understand what's going on, and so I know exactly what to do. And so the reasoning system has really expanded the capabilities of these cars. So the robo-taxis are here. What about humanoid robots? Right around the corner. And the reason for that is if you could imagine this—you guys know this—if you could prompt a video generator, and so ChatGPT has a new excellent video generator, and you say, "I want to generate somebody picking up a coffee cup and taking a sip," it would generate my hand picking up the coffee cup, knows exactly where the coffee cup is, and picks it up. If I can generate a video doing that, why can't I generate the robot to do it? All right, and so, I think you could imagine based on this, the other capability must be around the corner. And so, do you think this is like three to five years, or one to three years? What's the timeframe you envision these robots really becoming more mainstream than we are seeing right now? The big challenge right now is partly the AI model, but a lot is associated with the Megatronics. The motors, the hands, the structure—you want to be on the one hand light, on the other hand it has to be strong. And so, you want it to be light because if something were to happen and it tips over, having something that weighs 80 pounds tipping on you is probably okay; having something that weighs 300 pounds tipping on you probably isn't. And so, there's a very big difference between the current state of the art versus where we need to be, and we're going to get there. And so material science matters, motors matter, battery technology matters, sensors, and so all of these things matter and then of course there's the AI itself. And when do you think Olaf will become available at Target? I'm not hoping for Olaf, but you know the other robots that I have on stage usually with me from Disney—you know, they're incredibly cute. Who doesn't want their own R2-D2 growing up with them? Let's move to another serious topic. We're in Washington, Jensen. We've gotta talk China and export controls. You have been a proponent of us selling chips to China. There's a big criticism, there's a big opposition to that point about us not enabling our main competitor to get ahead of us. How do you address that criticism? The easiest way to think about that is to remember that AI is a five-layer cake. And the United States, America, should win in every aspect of AI. We should be the world leader in energy production and energy technology production. We should be the world leader in chips, we should be the world leader in infrastructure, we should be the world leader in models, and we absolutely cannot afford to lose this one: to be the world leader in AI application and AI adoption. The first thing that we should do is we should enable and encourage every single one of the layers of that cake and all the industries associated. Go lead the world, go lead in technology, go lead in market share, go lead in economic share, and go lead in the diffusion of our technology across the world so that everybody depends on the American tech stack. And so whether it's energy export or it's chip export, or it's infrastructure export or model export or application technology export, let's make sure that we're the world leader in every one of them. Let's export like crazy. As we know, we would love to have an incredible trade imbalance—meaning extraordinary amounts of exporting. And so why shouldn't we have AI, also, be in that camp? Now of course, we also want to make sure that from a technology perspective that we favor the American tech stack—meaning whenever we can, let's favor the American companies that are part of that stack. And so we should always have the best technology first and most to American companies. And we do. And so, you can find this balance: on the one hand, provide the best and the most to the United States; on the other hand, ensure that American companies win around the world. Yeah. The argument, Jensen, there is that across the five-layer cake, there is one particular layer that it's too important because in the others China can get ahead: they have cheaper energy, they have incredible talent so they can build—I mean they build already incredible applications, and adoption is probably much more popular there than here. So the compute becomes the essential node according to Washington—or some people in Washington—that you can slow them down. Not completely a zero-sum game, but at least they cannot catch up with where we are right now because if they do catch up and if it surpasses, then they'll become a global leader on probably what we have agreed is the most important technology of our lifetime. Every layer is important. Every layer is important. And we should make sure that we can accelerate the United States. Those two conditions, I think, both we could agree on: every single layer is important. The chip layer obviously is important. If the chip layer is not important, why are we talking about chips all the time? And so obviously the chip layer is incredibly important. Nvidia had, you know, call it 90-somewhat percent of the world's market share today; in China, we have now dropped to zero. Conceding an entire market the size of China probably doesn't make a lot of strategic sense. And so I think that that has already largely backfired. Maybe it made sense at the time, but I think the policy really needs to be dynamic and it has to stay with the times. At this point in time, I think it would be fairly safe to say that having American chip companies and other companies in China makes a lot of sense. I think that with respect to the model layer, we have to make sure that we do everything we can to help our model companies have the best, have the most, and have it first. And we do. And so, you know, this is the largest market in the world, we're the fastest market in the world, and the tech companies here are agile and they're innovative. I have every confidence that we're providing all the best here anyway. And so, I have every confidence that AI model companies here in the United States will continue to stay ahead. Yeah. I mean another thing would be that they will pursue self-sufficiency regardless of our policies because they have done it in almost every industry and they're pursuing it also in your industry. "Sorry." Well, they—let's see. They have self-sufficiency and pretty significant leadership in many layers. At the energy level, I think we can all acknowledge whether it's the production of energy or the technology that we use, they are the world leader. And so we are the world leader in chips. In AI models, I would say that we are ahead, we're unquestionably ahead; they're close behind. They have just such an extraordinary number of AI researchers. For whatever reason—it's because of interest in science and math and the encouragement of the social fabric—they just have such an extraordinary number of science and math experts. And as a result of that, the number of AI researchers in China is quite extraordinary; it's one of their national treasures, if you will—their greatest natural resource. And so we have to be mindful that we continue to attract that natural resource to the United States. We have to make sure that we welcome them here, that they want to come here. I'm quite frankly kind of concerned about many of them deciding to stay or not being allowed to leave. And so I think that, at the model layer, our technology is advanced, but we do deeply rely also on the talent that is around the world. And then lastly, at the highest level, my greatest concern—I remain very concerned today—we are so cinematic, so incredibly science fiction in the way we describe AI that we're causing so much consternation and so much fear around the United States. Meanwhile, the rest of Asia is embracing and adopting AI with great enthusiasm. And so, this is—this is something we have to be quite concerned about. This is how we get generally left behind. Yeah. At the GTC you talked about open source, and you touched upon OpenCLAW and how popular it is. I still remember that graph you showed, the downloads and everything. Open source is really incredibly important for AI democratization and accessibility and whatnot, but at the same time it brings safety and security issues. How does Nvidia address these things? So first of all, open source enhances safety and enhances security. The way that you're going to deal with a super agent cybersecurity attack is through a massive swarm of models that you adopted from open source, that you've trained to be a defense. A massive swarm of defense agents. And so you're not going to defend against a super agent with another super agent; you're going to defend it with massive swarms. And so you use asymmetry to your advantage, and that requires open source technology for us to develop upon. The CrowdStrikes, the Palo Alto Networks, the Ciscos, and the Microsofts all rely on open source technology to do that. Open source also has the benefit of being completely developed in sunlight, and so we understand the technology and we can keep it safe. And so safety and security is frankly enhanced with open source. With respect to OpenCLAW, it was such an incredible phenomenon. The concern that we had—which is the reason why we created, we invented two technologies. The first technology is OpenShell. We call it OpenShell because it's a shell, because it puts a shell around a lobster, you know, OpenCLAW. And so, the shell keeps it in a safe cage, if you will. And that technology was built at Nvidia as a starting point; we've contributed to open source, and it's being adopted by so many different companies. And the basic idea is to give it a virtual environment, a sandbox, and we're mindful about the type of information it can access and the policies that the policy engines will be able to control. The information that it can send in and out could be mindful about the privacy of personal information that's sent out. It could give you access to certain information that you cannot send out. And so all the policies associated with the privacy of each one of the instances of the Claw are now captured in OpenShell. And so we created an environment that makes it a lot more safe. Doing all of this completely in open source enables all of the world's enterprise companies to be able to adopt the technology because they understand what's underneath. And working with the US government and working with cybersecurity companies around the world, you know, this is how you're going to keep these agents safe. You touched on this really important topic throughout your conversation: AI adoption and the fear out there. Last time when you and I talked in July, we were both upbeat what the trajectory of this technology is going. But today, there are several elements that I think are boiling out there: you have the political discourse, you have the data center conversations, you have the state-level regulations, and you have the layoffs. Whether or not you can blame it on AI or the other things that have been happening from the tech companies, I'm afraid that we're entering this phase now where people might build a stigma around AI, which I think is not helpful because then you will have as a consequence a lack of AI adoption. How do you assess this? I mean you mentioned this throughout your conversation today that this is a big concern. There are things that are said that are very counterproductive and, in fact, hurtful. On the one hand, maybe a scientist thinks that by warning people that AI is going to completely permeate and proliferate across radiology and therefore radiologists are going to get wiped out—on the one hand that might be considered warning and therefore helpful, but in fact the counter would have been hurtful. If we convinced everybody not to be radiologists and we now need radiologists, that actually is hurtful to society. It is hurtful if we convince all the young college graduates to not be software engineers and it turns out the United States needs more software engineers than ever; that's hurtful. And so we have to be mindful of how we communicate the importance of this technology and what it's able to do. We have to advocate for policy and advocate for guardrails on the one hand; on the other hand, scaring people with nonsensical things which are not going to happen—that this is an existential threat to humanity, that there's a 20% chance that it's existential—that's ridiculous. That it's going to wipe out 50% of new college grad jobs, that it's going to completely destroy democracy. I mean these kinds of comments are not helpful. They're made by people who are like me: CEOs. And somehow because they became CEOs, you adopt a God complex and before you know it you know everything. And so I think we have to be careful and really ground ourselves in talking about the facts. The facts are this: AI has created more than half a million jobs in the last couple of years. The facts are AI is our best opportunity to re-industrialize the United States and to bring manufacturing jobs back to the United States. The facts are that's going to generate hundreds of thousands of jobs and trillions of dollars of new economy back into the United States. The facts of the matter are companies that use AI have demonstrated the ability to grow faster. When they grow faster, they hire more people. Apparently, AI creates jobs. And so the question is: why is it that on the one hand I'm telling you AI creates jobs, while on the other hand they say it destroys jobs? Well, the very simple idea is this: let's pretend for a second that the total lines of code that we have to write in the United States is one billion lines of code. And that one billion lines of code was supported by 10 million software developers and about a trillion dollars worth of the economy, and those one billion lines of code are now going to be automated because most of it is going to be written by AI. Well, if our job is basically typing and one billion lines of code are now automated away, you would come to the conclusion a trillion dollars worth of jobs will be destroyed. But that's fundamentally wrong for two reasons. The first reason is the task of our job and the purpose of our job are related, not the same. If you apply that to me, you would come to the conclusion what Jensen does for a living is tap on phones and talk. And tapping on phones and talking, AI has done that just fine, and therefore my job should be gone, but I'm busier than ever. And so there's a fundamental difference between the purpose of the job and the task of the job. And so that's one. The second thing is: it is a fundamental flaw that we only need a billion lines of code written. We need a trillion lines of code written. We need way more code written than that because we have the imagination of solving problems, whether it's in healthcare or science or manufacturing and retail. In all of the different fields that we have, we have lots and lots of imaginations of all the things that we can do. If we just didn't have to type anymore, we could go and do those things. And so maybe it's just because in the last 50 years society has taken this one little device with a keyboard on it and it consumed all of our lives to the point where we just can't imagine living without typing anymore. And so we'll figure it out, you guys. I mean, the idea that being human means to hunch over on this little thing typing all the time—you know, 50 years before that, people didn't do that. In the future we're going to do less of that and we're going to do more of something else. As you know we have the Expo next week, and I think part of the reason why we do the Expo is because we want people to see the prospect of this technology. And at the Expo people have the opportunity to build robots and to see what universities are doing and tech companies are doing with AI. So unfortunately we will miss you next week, but I know we worked with your team to have Nvidia's presence there. It's a big event for us. "The biggest, yes." We have lots and lots of people coming; it's such an important work and I love that you're celebrating AI through the lens of healthcare, energy, manufacturing, retail, and quantum. Different fields of science and different industries and different professions. And in all of those lenses, I think the people that have engaged with it are so incredibly excited. All the engineers at Nvidia today use AI. AI basically does most of our coding, and yet we're hiring more engineers than ever, we have more challenges than ever, we have bigger dreams than ever, and our ambitions are greater than ever. And so, if you just said this to yourself: suppose we infuse AI into this country and as a result of that, we are doing things faster than ever before. Our ambition is greater than ever before. Our expectations are greater than ever before. How is that a bad condition for our country? That's exactly what we want: to be more ambitious than ever, to be faster than ever, to be better than ever. And so, I hope that we adopt AI irrespective of anything else. Whatever we do, help people understand how AI has been transformative to so many people and so many industries already in just the last six months. In just the last six months since it's been really useful. Prior to that it's been incredible but not useful. Now it's useful and incredible. And so, I really do hope that every industry, every company takes advantage of it. President Trump signs an executive order expanding savings

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·11:33 ·365 views · 2026-04-30
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump signed a landmark executive order establishing the "Trump IRA" program, providing millions of low-income Americans access to low-cost retirement accounts with a federal matching contribution of up to $1,000 per year.
TL;DR President Trump signed a landmark executive order establishing the "Trump IRA" program, providing millions of low-income Americans access to low-cost retirement accounts with a federal matching contribution of up to $1,000 per year. The "Trump IRA" Initiative: Beginning...

TL;DR

President Trump signed a landmark executive order establishing the "Trump IRA" program, providing millions of low-income Americans access to low-cost retirement accounts with a federal matching contribution of up to $1,000 per year.

Expansion of Retirement Savings (Trump IRA)

  • The "Trump IRA" Initiative: Beginning next year, all Americans can access low-cost IRA accounts via trumpira.gov, modeled after the Thrift Savings Plan (TSP) currently available to federal employees.
      • Federal Savers Match: Low-income Americans (specifically those earning below $35,000) are eligible for up to $1,000 in annual federal matching funds deposited directly into their accounts.
      • Long-term Projections: The administration estimates that a 25-year-old investing $165 per month with the federal match could accumulate approximately $465,000 by age 65.
  • Phase Two Goals: Economic advisor Kevin Hassett noted that future legislative efforts will aim to expand this match to middle-income earners who lack employer-sponsored 401(k) plans.
  • Current Participation: The President noted that related retirement initiatives have already seen 5 million sign-ups, significantly exceeding initial expectations.

Economic Indicators and Manufacturing

  • Market Growth: President Trump highlighted that the stock market has set 59 all-time record highs during his tenure, adding an estimated $9 trillion in value to the U.S. economy.
  • 401(k) Comparisons: The President contrasted his administration's performance with the Biden administration, claiming the average 401(k) rose by $24,000 in his last year compared to $875 under his successor.
  • Manufacturing Resurgence: The briefing emphasized the return of automotive plants from Canada, Mexico, Germany, Japan, and South Korea, as well as U.S. leadership in AI development.

Energy and Infrastructure

  • Bridger Pipeline Authorization: The President signed a presidential permit for the Bridger pipeline, a trans-border project described as similar to the Keystone XL.
      • Strategic Goal: The permit is intended to enhance North American energy dominance and security by streamlining the movement of oil and gas.
      • Efficiency: The President argued that pipelines are a safer and more efficient alternative to transporting fuel via truck or train.

Federal Contracting and Administrative Reform

  • Shift to Fixed-Price Contracts: An executive order was signed to move federal contracting away from "cost-plus" models to a "fixed-price" default.
      • Accountability: This change aims to eliminate incentives for cost overruns and align government spending with private-sector practices to reduce fraud and abuse.
  • Surgeon General Nomination: The President officially withdrew the nomination of Casey Means and formally nominated Dr. Nicole Saphier for the position of U.S. Surgeon General.

National Proclamations

  • National Fallen Firefighters Memorial Weekend: A proclamation honoring the sacrifice of firefighters who have died in the line of duty.
  • National Foster Care Month: A proclamation recognizing the efforts of foster and adoptive parents, a key priority area for the First Lady.
"For millions of Americans who lack employer-sponsored plans, this will be really revolutionary because they'll be covered. Nobody thought that was possible."

— President Donald Trump

"What this executive order does is it establishes a fixed-price contract as the default in most federal contracts. This aligns the federal government with what would typically exist in the private sector and we believe will continue to drive down fraud and abuse."
