Sam Altman in conversation with Patrick Collison

Stripe(103.0K) ·58:20 ·29.9K views · 2026-04-30
Sam Altman (Cofounder and CEO, OpenAI) Patrick Collison (CEO, Stripe)
OpenAI is entering its third phase as a "mega-scale token factory for the world," aiming to become a low-margin, high-volume infrastructure utility that accelerates global economic output and scientific discovery.
TL;DR OpenAI is entering its third phase as a "mega-scale token factory for the world," aiming to become a low-margin, high-volume infrastructure utility that accelerates global economic output and scientific discovery. Sam Altman predicts that AI will soon move beyond coding to...

TL;DR

OpenAI is entering its third phase as a "mega-scale token factory for the world," aiming to become a low-margin, high-volume infrastructure utility that accelerates global economic output and scientific discovery. Sam Altman predicts that AI will soon move beyond coding to automate the "drudgery" of all computer-based work, while significantly compressing the timeline for breakthroughs in material science and biotechnology.

The Evolution of OpenAI’s Mission and Strategy

  • Three Phases of OpenAI: Sam Altman outlined the company’s structural trajectory:
      • Phase 1: A pure research lab focused on the "crazy" goal of building AGI.
      • Phase 2: The development of a product-focused company (ChatGPT, APIs).
      • Phase 3: A global "token factory" providing massive-scale intelligence as a new public utility.
  • The "Infrastructure Play": Altman views OpenAI’s future as an infrastructure provider similar to Stripe, favoring a low-margin, high-volume business model aligned with customer success.
      • Switching Costs: He notes that as AI gets smarter, switching costs between models actually decrease, making high-margin "moats" harder to maintain and further incentivizing the utility model.
  • Massive CapEx Requirements: Altman describes the current build-out as the most expensive infrastructure project in human history.
      • Demand vs. Price: He believes demand for intelligence is effectively uncapped, provided the price continues to drop and speed increases.
      • Bubble Concerns: While journalists frequently call a "CapEx bubble," Altman argues that the current scale of demand makes the massive investments look reasonable.

Product Frontiers: From Coding to "General Drudgery"

  • The Coding Threshold: The recent "parabolic" growth in AI utility was triggered by models hitting a reasoning threshold that made them effective for coding.
      • Codex and OpenClaw: These tools represent a shift where the AI becomes the primary interface for the computer.
      • Automation of Drudgery: The next major unlock isn't a specific domain like spreadsheets, but the automation of "switching between apps" and "copy-pasting"—the small tasks that break creative flow.
  • "Revenge of the Idea Guys": In the startup ecosystem, technical talent remains vital, but the barrier to entry has lowered.
      • Founder Traits: Altman is now more willing to fund "idea-driven" founders who deeply understand their users but may lack traditional coding skills, as AI handles more of the technical execution.
  • Headcount Projections: Despite the massive scale of the business, Altman expressed a desire to keep OpenAI’s headcount extremely lean, ideally only doubling from its current size over the next five years.

Science and the OpenAI Foundation

  • Accelerating Discovery: Altman believes the most important contribution of AI will be accelerating science, potentially achieving a decade’s worth of progress in a single year.
  • The OpenAI Foundation: This non-profit arm will be one of the largest foundations in the world, focusing on science and AI resilience.
      • Material Science: Altman identified this as the most underrated domain for AI impact, noting its potential to revolutionize catalysts and physical materials.
      • Biotech and Health: OpenAI is supporting efforts like the Arc Institute to tackle complex diseases (e.g., Alzheimer’s) through foundation models like Evo-2.
  • Energy Breakthroughs: Altman made the bold prediction that the world may see a profitable nuclear fusion reactor within the next five years, driven partly by the massive energy demands of AI data centers.

Management Style and Philosophy

  • Hands-Off Leadership: Altman describes himself as a non-hands-on manager who hires great people, gives them a high-level goal, and stays out of the way.
      • Context Gathering: He maintains a "pulse" on the company by communicating with hundreds of employees daily via Slack/text to gather diffuse context.
  • Managing Elite Talent: The "magic" of OpenAI was getting highly capable, often iconoclastic individuals to work together long enough to reach breakthroughs. This was achieved through a shared, deep conviction in "scale" and concentrating resources on a single research program.
  • Iterative Deployment vs. Power Concentration: Altman defended OpenAI’s decision to release tools like ChatGPT early and often.
      • He rejects the "ivory tower" approach where a small group of experts controls the technology, arguing instead for democratized access so the world can build on it.
"We have to figure out how to build this like mega, mega scale token factory for the world... we need to make that as smart, as cheap, as abundant, as easy to use as possible." — Sam Altman
"I think we're going to try this new experiment where we start sending like an FTE to work... hands-on with the CEO of a company to automate their job as much of it as they can." — Sam Altman
"The most important contribution of AI... is that if we can start to discover new science at a much faster rate... life gets better because we understand science better." — Sam Altman
Please welcome back Stripe co-founder and CEO Patrick Collison. Good evening, folks. I really hope you've enjoyed the announcements and the sessions over the course of the day thus far. I am extremely excited about this interview. As you know, I was due to be interviewing Greg Brockman, who was a very early Stripe employee and then a co-founder of OpenAI. But AI is a dynamic space and there is a lot happening, so we've made a slight substitution. Instead, I'm going to be interviewing somebody that I've known for a long time. I was just counting backstage, and I think I've known him for 18, maybe 19 years. He was actually one of the very earliest investors in Stripe—I think Stripe's second or third investor—and he also co-founded OpenAI in 2015. Please welcome to the stage, Sam Altman. All right, I think we have some Codex fans in the audience. Love to hear that. Sam, how's the week going? It is so fun, but it is a busy week. I'm happy to be here. This was an unexpected surprise. Well, thank you for joining us; we appreciate it. We opened this morning by saying that we've kind of arbitrarily decided that the singularity started on January 1st, and thus, today is day 119. What do you think of that? It does feel like we are somehow in the takeoff. Day 119 feels like a reasonable enough guess. Yeah, I won't fight it. Did you feel this? We've started to see a bunch of our metrics inflect as of late last year and the beginning of this year. Things were kind of doing well, but somehow the shapes of the curves changed and really went parabolic. Is that matched in what you guys see? Was there some trajectory change? I do think the models got really good, especially for coding, but really good in general starting late last year and very early this year. In my own experience of using the technology and seeing what other people are doing with it, there is this sense that every week is now a little bit different than the week before. Just a lot happens very fast. It seemed to all correlate with the models hitting some threshold. Why did coding models suddenly start to click over the last couple of months? Was there a research trick, or was it that you just got enough code data in the pre-training? It's a great question. We've wondered a lot about why several people crossed that threshold at the same time. I'm sure it's a number of factors, including raw reasoning horsepower. It was enough of a feedback loop of people using it for code to figure out where it was good and where it needed to improve. I think it was all of these things. Also, like many other endeavors, once you know something is possible, it's much easier to go do it with vigor. It seems like Codex is kind of having a moment right now. It really crossed some subjective threshold for me with the latest app updates in 5.5. It is quite hard to say why right now and not a little bit sooner, or why not the next model. One of the things I have learned about the history of all the things we've put out is it is very hard to say why this particular thing was the thing that worked. This goes back to ChatGPT. Why was GPT-3.5 the thing that got over the threshold where most people went from saying "not that impressive" to "gonna change the world"? Why not one model earlier or later? I really can't explain it. You just kind of feel it. I've had two inflection points with Codex. One was kind of with GPT-5.2 and then a really big one in the last few weeks where it's like, "Okay, this is going to be the primary interface to a computer for me." Is everyone using it for coding, or are you starting to see usage diffuse into other domains? The most adamant users are still using it for coding, but there's been this tidal wave of people coming into Codex recently. I'm really trying to understand what has happened to cause this. The depth of what people are starting to use it for has surprised me. Certainly, our ambition is for it not just to be about coding, but to be about all the work you do in front of a computer. I would say we're maybe 10 percent of the way there for the non-coding stuff, but now that we see what's happening and we have a real user base using it in these other ways, I think we'll get good at it very fast. What do you think will be the next domain that subjectively feels like it has this big unlock after coding? Is it going to be spreadsheets, or performance reviews? I do think coding is a little bit special and these models are a great fit for it. The world needs so much more code than currently gets written. There may be no other domain that is quite like coding, but there will be a lot of others that I think are close. The next coding-like thing that I think will happen is not any specific domain, but the realization of how much time people waste trying to use a computer. There is an idea that you can do a huge percentage of your day in a very different way. You don't realize how much time you spend clicking between messaging apps and copying and pasting stuff. Responding to very boring things that you could clearly automate once is the key. The degree to which most people will realize they can sit back and watch an AI do most of their drudgery is going to surprise people. In my own experience trying to work that way, it actually gives me much more enjoyment of work. I didn't realize how much the little stuff drags me down or gets me out of a happy flow state. So the subjective quality of life improvement is huge. Are you an OpenClaw user? I am. Any OpenClaw users here? We have some good news for you all coming. You can just tell them. OpenClaw has been one of my biggest "this is magic AGI" moments ever in the field. I remember the first time someone told me about it. They were trying to explain it and I was like, "Okay, that all sounds cool, but I can make a lot of that work." Then it was a real reminder of what happens when the models cross some threshold. When the product designer gets a handful of critical ideas really right, it's a much more magical experience than it sounds like. I find I've been an attempting OpenClaw evangelist, trying to describe that experience to others, and I find it difficult to communicate. It sounds kind of prosaic: a stateful ChatGPT session that can also make some use of tools. What do you use OpenClaw for? If we scrolled your message thread, what would we see? This is a very embarrassing thing to admit. You're in the perfect place. The thing I always try first with a new kind of AI system is to try to build a better home automation interface because it never works. It's never as good. OpenClaw was the first time that I was able to get a setup that I was happy with. I also built a messaging app that I had always wanted to work. I've since switched it to something I built with Codex, but OpenClaw was the first time I was able to automate the unpleasant task of waking up and going through a drowning amount of messages. That should have been doable with previous systems, but it's hard to explain what it's like when it all actually just works and you trust that it's going to work. I was testing the new Link CLI that we just launched today and I asked my agent to buy itself a gift—anything on the internet for under 20 dollars. It chose to buy itself an HTTP zine from Gumroad. Wow. There is all of the stuff that feels strange, no matter how convinced you are intellectually that this is not a real thing wanting a real gift for itself. No matter how much you're convinced that this is a weird emergent behavior and you're not supposed to read into it, there are things that feel a little strange. We're going to have a party for GPT-5.5. I wasn't quite sure what to do, so on a whim, I asked 5.5 what it would like for a party for itself this morning. It gave this beautiful set of things, including what it would want for the flow of the party and what it would not want. It suggested we should do it on May 5th because that would be funny. It wanted only a short little toast, and it wanted the toast not to be by itself, but by the people that built it. It wanted a big central suggestion for 5.6. It wanted me to feed those suggestions into it. Now there's real moral pressure on you, but it was a strange thing. I want to ask you about OpenAI itself. It is now a 10-year-old organization. It somehow feels like so much longer than that, but yes, just over 10. A long 10 years. I can't remember pre-OpenAI life that well at this point; it feels like it's been so long. You have a singularity looking forward but also a singularity looking backwards. What's the craziest OpenAI story that's never been told? It sounds so prosaic relative to the crazy drama that's happened, but there was this period after we had finished training GPT-4. There were about eight months before we released it. There was this eight-month period inside of OpenAI where we were all using this thing. We kind of knew that it was dramatically better and different and was going to unlock a bunch of things in the world. No one outside the company or almost no one outside the company knew about it. We'd walk the halls sometimes and wonder if we were engaging in collective psychosis. We wondered if we had just totally whipped each other into this frenzy. There was no feedback to keep us in check or sane from the outside world. It doesn't sound that weird relative to crazy board drama or the Elon trial, but living through it was an unbelievably strange time. What's the Sam Altman management style? If I'm working for you directly, what does that look like? I'm definitely not a hands-on manager. I'm very much of the style that you get great people, give them a very high-level thing to point at, and try to let stuff just happen. I think there have been two main phases of OpenAI and we're heading into a third. The first one was when we were only a research company. We were trying to figure out how we were going to build AGI at a time when it sounded completely crazy and we really had no idea what to do. Then there was a second phase where, in addition to continuing to do that, we had to figure out how to build a product company. Now we have a third phase. We have to figure out how to build this mega-scale token factory for the world. I think of what we're doing as building sort of a new utility. People are going to want to use a lot of tokens and a lot of intelligence in all sorts of ways. We need to make that as smart, as cheap, as abundant, and as easy to use as possible. That will require pretty deep full-stack integration and a massive infrastructure build-out. The thing that I didn't really appreciate between the phase one and phase two shift was how much my management style had to change. Running a research lab and running a product company are two extremely different things. I suspect this third phase is going to be very different yet again, so I've been reflecting on how I'll have to change. I think it's not going to be a natural fit for my management style. I either have to find someone great to hire, or figure out how to do things in a different way, or build an AI that can manage this new thing. When I interviewed Jensen here two years ago, he told me about his 60 direct reports. Do you have any unusual practices like that? The closest thing I have is that I probably talk to a few hundred people at the company a day via Slack or text. It's very quick, one or two messages, not done by an agent. I actually do it myself. The context I get from that sometimes is very helpful in these diffuse ways. I find there's an interesting watershed of pre-Slack organizations and post-Slack organizations, and they're truly quite different. I hate Slack, but I can't imagine having to still communicate via email or whatever we used to. There's a view that the AI labs are going to progress up the stack, gobbling up the value chain voraciously. There is a fear that there will be this incredible positive feedback loop and a hegemonic force that we should all be getting very concerned about. What's your view? I think some of them do want that, but we don't. One of the things that I have always admired about Stripe is it is very clear that Stripe is aligned with its customers. We make more revenue, we charge our customers more. Thank you, by the way, for the partnership with Stripe when ChatGPT launched. It was extremely critical, and I don't think anyone else could have scaled that quickly. But we scaled, we pay you more money, it's very aligned and we're all happy. You just provide a layer of infrastructure for the internet. I don't know exactly how to do this yet, but I would like to get to a model for OpenAI that is similar. I would like us to be an infrastructure provider. I'd be happy for us to be a forever low-margin business as long as we can be huge and growing fast. I would like us to supply an intelligence meter that companies can buy to automate things or build products. People can take it with them and we find ways to align ourselves with the success of the entire economic engine of the world. I believe that will work. I believe that switching costs of AI are going to make it hard to have huge margins anyway. You've seen recently how easy it is for people to switch from our competitor's coding product to ours. This is a consequence of AI getting smarter; it gets easier to just say, "Hey agent, go do this thing for me." So many people have been explicitly critical of OpenAI for procuring so much compute. You were quite noteworthy for stipulating what sounded like preposterous figures for the magnitude of the build-out required. Obviously, the preposterousness of those figures now looks less tenuous by the day. Thoughts on compute CapEx and the build-out? It's going to take a lot of money. I think this will clearly be the most expensive infrastructure project that the world has ever undertaken. The revenue is ramping to meet it, so people feel better about that. Also, the efficiency gains that we've all been finding are incredible. We're going to get way more out of each GPU than I thought we were going to. As it has often been remarked, the demand goes up more than linearly as you drop the price of each unit of intelligence. This is especially true if you can drop the price and the speed with which you get it back. The question now of "what is enough" is something I don't have a good answer to. In some sense, I think demand for intelligence at a low enough price is effectively uncapped. We're not going to build the Dyson sphere and then just cover it with data centers, but maybe we do. Space data centers? Good luck with that. I don't even think he's that serious about it. I don't myself think that we're in a CapEx bubble. I'm not an expert in this, but the figures I see relative to the magnitude of the demand look reasonable to me. People love to proclaim bubbles. I can't articulate why, but intellectually I kind of get it. It feels fun and it feels smart. Journalists, in particular, love to talk about bubbles. So there's ample desire to write about this whenever anything looks a little bit silly. Clearly, sometimes it's right. But how you can discern between the amount of time someone calls a bubble and the amount of time you're actually in a bubble, I have never figured out how to do. In my previous career, I was an investor, so I was quite interested in trying to see if I could come up with some sort of framework for this. I never was able to figure it out. I read what smart people had said at different points in history and they called it exactly right, but they also said it 10 more times in the 10 previous years. Your business OpenAI depends in a very significant way on super talented people. These effective people are not in every case super easy to work with. Some are wonderful people, and some are the most fantastic collaborators, but some are very iconoclastic and strong-willed and they get easily irked. How do you think about managing elite skill here? Do you tolerate prima donnas? Someone working on a book about OpenAI once told me that I figured out how to get a lot of people, who all thought they were the only capable person, to work together long enough to figure out the breakthroughs. What's the trick? A lot of pain. I think we had very deeply shared convictions even when people didn't like each other. We collectively believed in scale and concentrating resources on this one thing. We thought getting this right was important enough that people were going to put aside various personal conflicts. One of the most unusual things about OpenAI was that at the time we trained GPT-3, the vast majority of our compute was going into this one single research program. We would talk to people that we were trying to recruit from DeepMind, and they would say that's insane and that it was going to create a terrible culture. John and I have been doing this thing at Stripe since 2010. You and Greg started out in 2015 and you've ventured through many trials and stratospheric successes. It's not easy to work successfully with a co-founder for a decade. Why has that partnership worked? How have you made it such a success? Obviously, you and John knew each other for longer than Greg and I did, but Greg and I did know each other for a long time before OpenAI. I think having the shared history really helps. One of the things that I had observed at Y Combinator was that one of the biggest predictors of success was how long the co-founders had known each other. The teams that came together seven days before applying to YC on a co-founder matching site rarely worked. It was not impossible, but it was rare. We had known each other for a while and had a sense of shared values. We were clear on what we wanted to do. We had this deep mutual respect and complementary skill set that has just worked really well. I'm extremely grateful. I think having to go through any startup experience without a co-founder you have a deep connection to is really hard. I've watched people do it, but it's very hard. There's this entire ecosystem of companies building on the platform. It's an interesting moment given the ability to build products at unprecedented rates. You are one of the most prolific and successful startup investors ever. Have the traits that make founders successful changed in this era? There was a time when we used to make fun of the "idea guy." These people wanted to start a company and say, "I have the best idea, I just need a coder to build it for me." It was kind of always personally annoying to me because it's like saying, "I have a great idea for a song, I just need that guy with the guitar to make it for me." YC had a version of this where teams without technical founders were difficult to make work. All of a sudden, it's like the revenge of the idea guys, which is actually awesome for the world. For a long time, the most important ingredient we looked for was technical talent. Now, people who deeply understand their users and can't code at all—I want to fund those people. How does one think about startup investing these days? You have a couple of years potentially to AGI or the singularity, and then you have funds with 10-year time horizons. How does that all fit together? I think to do anything at this point on a 10-year time horizon requires a real suspension of disbelief. And yet, that's probably the right way to live your life. I don't think it works to say there's this singularity in three years or five years and we can't see past it, so we're going to do nothing. You have to live as if stuff is just going to keep going in an understandable way for a long time. How far ahead does OpenAI plan? I mean, we find 20-year power and land agreements for infrastructure. For the product, I think we have a clear vision of what things can look like in two years, and then it gets much hazier after that. There was a narrative that GPT wrappers were undifferentiated and flimsy and would be swept away by model improvements. Now it feels like that narrative is flipping. Instead of talking about wrappers, we talk about harnesses, and harnesses are seen as having significant heft. What's your view on their durability? I have kind of had the same view all the way through, which is you as a business want to be on the side of hoping that AI gets smarter. In the early model days, if you were a GPT wrapper patching a weakness in the current model, when the next model was much better, you were kind of sad. If you were doing something that benefited from intelligence, you would be happier. I think the same thing applies to harnesses. The right way to think about this is that the data center, model, and harness is just one cluster out of which comes usable intelligence. There are so many things to go build where you're just happier for that whole cluster to get better. When you look at the organizations making the most effective use of AI today, what specifically are they doing that's different? Tobi Lütke of Shopify was the first CEO I knew that just said we are going to be all-in on AI in the way we run our company. He got his hands dirty just building AI automation of everything. It was not a token leaderboard or some gamified thing. It was the CEO saying we are now going to put AI into everything we do, and then really holding people to it. That energy has now been done by other people, but when the leader of a company really holds people to it and ideally does it themselves, that has worked very well. We're going to try this new experiment where we start sending an FTE to work hands-on with the CEO of a company to automate their job. That will have a nice fractal effect throughout the company. A second thing is being uncomfortably permissive with data access. There are huge reasons not to do this, and I'm stopping short of a recommendation, but it's what I've seen from the most effective companies. It's easier for small startups than companies that have a lot of sensitive data. Saying you are going to record your meetings and let the AI have access to the code base and every Slack message is very powerful. I don't know how the world is going to decide the tradeoffs on data privacy versus AI efficiency. I think there is some regulation that's going to have to change for that, but it is so powerful. Where is open source AI going? Does it have a future? For sure. Right now people want smarter and cheaper frontier intelligence, but there is also a lot of demand for open source. I know science is something you're very excited about. OpenAI recently made a grant to the Arc Institute. Can you speak a little bit to AI as applied to science and how you think about grant-making in the context of the OpenAI Foundation? I hope that this will be the most important contribution of this technology. If we can start to discover new science at a much faster rate, life gets better. Starting with the models of a few months ago, but really now with 5.5, the models have gotten smart enough that excellent scientists are saying they are able to figure out better ideas. The pace of science is going to increase. Eventually, we'll have automated labs and robots. If we can start doing a decade of science in a year, the compounding effect will be extremely great. This will be one of the big areas of focus of the OpenAI Foundation—money, expertise, and technology to accelerate science. I think this will be the biggest foundation in the world. We're really focused on science and then AI resilience, which means helping the world through this transition. We were thrilled to support Arc. I think it's clearly the best AI and bio effort. If we can make even a small contribution to treat diseases, we will be very thrilled. I thought that was going to take longer, and looking at the incredible work the Arc Foundation is doing, I now think maybe it won't be that far off. This is your interstitial ad for the Arc Institute. There is an Arc Institute booth downstairs. The Arc Institute is an organization we started four years ago. Its goal is to produce the first cure for a complex disease in humans, like most cancers, autoimmune diseases, or neurodegenerative diseases. Humanity has never cured a complex disease, not one. We started Arc Institute with this goal, and Alzheimer's is the first complex disease that we're targeting. Our hope is that with new genome engineering technologies like CRISPR and amazing advances in AI, we'll be able to make some meaningful progress. The early results are very encouraging. The Arc Institute is currently looking for a CTO. We had one CTO do a sabbatical at Arc last year, and he helped us train Evo-2. But we're looking for a full-time CTO and we thought that person might be in this audience. If you know somebody for whom that sounds of interest, go check out the Arc stand downstairs. That was good. It's so much better that you label that as the interstitial ad rather than just doing it. You were the second investor in Stripe. YC and Paul were in that Palo Alto kitchen. We were two pimply teenagers proposing building a financial services institution. Why did you invest? Honestly, because you and John were two of the most impressive founders of any age that I had ever met. I clearly was right about that. I had known of you on the internet. I was struck that you were solving a problem for yourself and that it fit a trend. I really believed commerce was going to move online in a huge way. If you can find really smart founders and a market that's going to be big, you should just invest. What's your advice for Stripe on navigating the AI era? I remember when investors would outsmart themselves saying Stripe's going to be a commodity. It turns out that if you make a great product, people are still going to need to take money in the future. They are probably just going to stick with you if you're a good ecosystem partner. Every company needs to get more efficient with AI. But this whole mindset that every company is going to go away and everything is going to be completely different—maybe it will be agents that need to handle payments instead of consumers, but money's going to have to move somehow. Adopt AI especially internally, and use AI to build better products, but don't assume that the entire socioeconomic system completely reconfigures. I think the world has gotten a little bit delusional about this. If we index OpenAI headcount to 100 today, what do you think OpenAI headcount is in five years? I would love it to be 200. Apart from AI, what technologies excite you? I'm obsessed now with data center infrastructure. There's so much cool stuff to do there. I think there will be amazing new technologies at the physical layer, including energy and robots. That stack is probably what I think about the most. The world is finally making a little progress on brain-machine interfaces. I'm excited about that, and I'm excited about progress in biotech. I'm excited about new kinds of computer interfaces. We are in an insane era right now where we're stuck with these old devices and old operating systems. When do you think we'll have the world's first profitable nuclear fusion reactor? It depends how far electricity prices get pushed by data center demand. Maybe sooner than we thought. I'll guess in the next five years. That's a bold prediction. Hypersonic commercial air travel? I don't know, more than 10 years. Are there any domains of science and technology not in the discourse that you think will be accelerated by AI? Material science. It's not a cool thing and I think people underestimate how much of the world is materials. It's a beautifully AI-shaped problem, getting new catalysts. I expect rapid progress there that will impact all of our lives. How do you hope your involvement changes the trajectory for the world? I believe in democratization and personal agency and that everybody deserves a really great life. The most controversial decision we made in the history of OpenAI was what we now call iterative deployment. A lot of the thinking when we released ChatGPT was that this is insanely dangerous to do. People thought only a small set of people should know what's coming. That sat very poorly with me. I believe it is extremely important that we avoid that kind of power concentration and that we build this for the world. By enabling people to explore this very wide opportunity space, we will give the world a gift. The world will build a much bigger gift on top of it. I am a believer in entrepreneurship and that people are mostly good and do amazing things with tools. My biggest contribution will be pushing for this to be a democratized technology. Sam Altman, thank you very much. Thank you for joining us.

This is AGI: Sequoia AI Ascent 2026 Keynote

Sequoia Capital(163.0K) ·32:27 ·16.5K views · 2026-04-30
Pat Grady (Partner, Sequoia Capital) Sonya Huang (Partner, Sequoia Capital) Konstantine Buhler (Partner, Sequoia Capital)
Artificial Intelligence has transitioned from a revolution in communication to a revolution in computation, shifting the focus from "faster horses" (incremental productivity) to "cars" (long-horizon agents).
TL;DR Artificial Intelligence has transitioned from a revolution in communication to a revolution in computation, shifting the focus from "faster horses" (incremental productivity) to "cars" (long-horizon agents). This shift is transforming software into a $10 trillion services...

TL;DR

Artificial Intelligence has transitioned from a revolution in communication to a revolution in computation, shifting the focus from "faster horses" (incremental productivity) to "cars" (long-horizon agents). This shift is transforming software into a $10 trillion services market where cognitive labor is being commoditized at an unprecedented scale.

The Paradigm Shift: From Communication to Computation

Pat Grady (Partner, Sequoia) characterizes the current AI wave not as a tool for distributing information, but as a fundamental shift in how information is processed.

  • A Revolution in Computation: Unlike the internet or mobile waves, which focused on communication, AI is a "revolution in computation." This creates a "moving floor" where the technological foundation changes daily.
  • Three Inflection Points:
      • Pre-training: Validated by the "ChatGPT moment" in late 2022.
      • Reasoning: The emergence of inference-time scaling (e.g., OpenAI’s o1).
      • Long-Horizon Agents: The arrival of agents that can persist until a job is done (e.g., Claude Code), which Grady defines as "functional AGI."
  • The "Car" Analogy: Previous AI applications were "faster horses" (10-40% productivity gains). The new era of agents represents "cars"—tools that offer 10-40x productivity and fundamentally change the nature of organizations.
  • The $10 Trillion Opportunity: AI is moving beyond the $650B software market into the multi-trillion dollar services market. For example, legal services in the US alone represent a $400B vertical that can now be addressed by AI.

The MAD Strategy for AI Builders

Grady offers the MAD framework for founders building on top of foundation models to navigate a market where "no lead is safe."

  • Moats (Customer-Back Approach): In a world where product capabilities change instantly, moats are built by wrapping technology around the customer. While technology is vital, customer intimacy is more durable than specific features.
  • Affordance (Path of Least Resistance): Borrowing from design, "affordance" means creating tools that are intuitive. While a terminal (like Claude Code) is powerful, builders must create interfaces that are "brain-dead simple" for the average enterprise user.
  • Diffusion (The Capability Gap): The "diffusion gap" is the space between what labs can do and what Fortune 500 companies actually use. This gap represents the primary opportunity for application-layer startups.

The Rise of the Agentic Economy

Sonya Huang (Partner, Sequoia) argues that "services is the new software," as agents move from "cute but useless" experiments to autonomous workers.

  • The Agentic Scale: AI is progressing through four stages of autonomy:

1. AI Assistance: Tab auto-complete and inline help.

2. Agentic Development: Humans instructing and managing agents.

3. Async Agents: Background agents spawning sub-agents with minimal human oversight.

4. Dark Factories: High-leverage systems where human review is removed entirely (already appearing in cybersecurity).

  • Tokens vs. Salaries: Hiring agents is infinitely more scalable and cheaper than hiring humans. Tasks that previously required expensive salaries are being replaced by token costs.
  • Vertical Impact:
      • Medicine: Agents inspecting genomes and prescribing medication.
      • Law: Agents performing litigation and settling contracts.
      • Science: Agents discovering new superconductors or solving complex mathematical problems.
  • Compressed Timelines: The ability to stack AI-driven breakthroughs means that "whatever you could imagine building over the next 100 years is now possible in 100 days."

The Cognitive Industrial Revolution

Konstantine Buhler (Partner, Sequoia) predicts that AI will do for cognitive work what the Industrial Revolution did for physical labor.

  • 99.9% Machine Cognition: Just as 99% of physical work is now done by machines (planes, combustion engines), Buhler predicts nearly all cognition will eventually be machine-led.
  • The Commoditization of Intelligence:
      • The Aluminum Analogy: In the 1800s, aluminum was more precious than gold. Once electrolysis made it easy to extract, it became disposable. Intelligence is currently "aluminum"—a precious skill that is about to become a cheap, instantly invoked commodity.
  • Alien Design: When machines take over design, the results will be "alien" and non-intuitive to humans. Buhler cites a NASA antenna designed by an evolutionary algorithm that outperformed human designs but lacked any recognizable geometric symmetry.
  • The Science of AI: We are currently in a "tinkering phase" similar to early steam engine development. Buhler predicts the emergence of a formal science of AI—comparable to thermodynamics—within the next few decades.
  • The Human Measure: As AI masters logic and realism, the value of work will shift toward human connection and the "Art of Unreason." Relationships and emotional experiences will become the primary measures of value.

Notable Quotes

"Functional AGI is when you can dispatch an agent to do a job, and it can recover from failure and persist until that job is done." — Pat Grady
"The last two decades that we spent building tools for humans have ended up being able to port over to be incredibly useful for agents as well... SaaS is not dead; its value will explode as the number of agents using it increases." — Sonya Huang
"We're about to enter a world where some of the most precious skills that took decades to earn—PhD-level skills—are so instantly invoked that right after using them, you can crumple them up and throw them right in the trash." — Konstantine Buhler
"You cannot pass 15 cars in the sun, but you can pass 15 cars in the rain. And right now, there is a torrential downpour of new capabilities." — Pat Grady
Good morning. How's everybody doing? Alright. Alright. A little bit better. Hey, thank you all for being here. We really appreciate it. We do this as a service to the community because we are living through important times, and it's an honor for us to be able to serve as a bit of a gathering place for people to come together. This is by far the best agenda we have put together and by far the best set of attendees. And so we just want to start out by saying thank you. We know you are all crazy busy. Thank you for being here today. So we do have a pretty exceptional agenda put together as per usual. Sonya is here in the impeccable outfit. Konstantine and I are going to say a couple of words to start. You know, we have the privilege of being in the midst of a lot of conversations with a lot of interesting people. And so once a year we like to try to synthesize that a little bit and share back with you what it is we've been hearing. I'll say a few words of overall calibration, and then Sonya will say a bit about what we see today, and then Konstantine will say a bit about what we think might be coming tomorrow. So for calibration, we're going to start by zooming out, going back to the silicon-based transistors which gave this area its name. That got built into systems connected by networks that went public in the form of the internet, supported applications like social media and the cloud, and eventually showed up in our pockets in mobile devices that today are capable of doing something indistinguishable from magic, which is AI. The reason we like to show this slide, and those of you who have been here in the past have seen this before, is because it reminds us that all of these waves are additive. We sort of needed all of these decades of evolution to have the compute, the bandwidth, the data, and the talent to make the most of this moment. Now this AI wave is a little bit different in three ways. First, it's the biggest wave yet. And that's generally true, but there are some things more specifically true about this wave, which is that it is the first one that is both software and services. The top row shows the first 15 years of the cloud transition where the TAM for software went from about 350 billion to 650 billion, and cloud grew to be about 400 billion of that. The bottom row is what is brand new. This is the services revenue that seems to also be available now. Ten trillion dollars is a conveniently round number. We don't know if it's 10 trillion or 5 trillion or 50 trillion. We do know that legal services in the US alone is a 400 billion dollar market. That is one vertical in one geo and it's the same as all of software. So this opportunity is immense. Point number two: this is the fastest wave yet. I think we can all feel this. What it means is that this whitespace, and I direct your attention to the AI side of this page, this whitespace is getting filled pretty fast. These logos are the companies that got to a billion plus of revenue as a result of the cloud, mobile, and now AI tectonic shifts. And at current course and speed, there are more coming soon. Point number three, which is probably the most interesting one, and I borrow this from my partner Konstantine, is that there are two basic kinds of revolutions in technology. There are revolutions of communication, which are about the way information is distributed. Most of the people in this room have only lived through revolutions in communication. The internet, the cloud, mobile—those are all about information distribution. Those were revolutions in communication. AI is different. AI is this one. AI is a revolution in computation. It's about how information is processed. And that might sound like semantics, but these are fundamentally different shapes of waves. And maybe the most visceral way to feel this is to think about the fact that the floor keeps moving underfoot. The technology foundation on which everybody builds changes every day when new capabilities come out. And we've had three major inflection points over the last handful of years. First one: the ChatGPT moment in November 2022. The world saw the power of pre-training. Second one, a couple years later: the o1 model and reasoning. All of a sudden a second scaling law emerges around inference time compute. Third one: just recently, with Claude Code and Opus 4.5, now 4.7. The world saw the power of long-horizon agents. And while these look like three points on a continuum, it's kind of a hard break between two and three. It's a little bit of a discontinuous change. And if we may be so bold, we would say that this is AGI. And look, I'm an econ major, we're venture capitalists, not about to propose a technical definition for AGI, okay? We study founders and markets and the collision thereof, which is businesses. But we do study businesses. And so from a commercial standpoint, from a practical standpoint, from a functional standpoint, if you can dispatch an agent to do a job, and it can recover from failure and persist until that job is done, I don't know, that feels pretty much like AGI. Even if you don't think it's AGI, which is fine, by the way Sonya will talk a lot more about this in her part. Even if you don't think it's AGI, I think we can all see that the cars have arrived. Last few years we've had a lot of faster horses. Applications that made you 10 or 40 percent more productive but didn't fundamentally change the way you work. Now we're starting to see cars. Applications that make you 10 or 40X more productive and absolutely change the way that you work, change the nature of your work, change the nature of your organization. Cars have arrived. This is the founder of Sequoia, Don Valentine. He was famous for asking one question: "So what?" Why does all this stuff matter? Well, it matters because just in the last few months, the race has begun. And it's a different kind of race than what we're used to. The way you drive a car is different than the way you ride a horse. The way you build a car is different than the way you take care of a horse. So it's a very different sort of race. And one of the reasons that we wanted to gather everybody here today is because nobody has all the answers. And the more time we can spend together, the more we can learn and hopefully figure out where all this stuff is headed. And it's important that we do so as soon as possible because there's a lot at stake. Just from a commercial perspective, there's 10 trillion dollars up for grabs. We got labs coming at it from a tech-out approach. We got startups building on top, coming at it from more of a customer-back approach. We do have all of the labs represented in this room, but most of you are building on top. And so we'll take a minute and talk about that customer-back approach. So our advice for those of you who are building on top of the labs, this is free advice and so it's worth every penny you paid for it. Our advice would be to get MAD. And we don't actually need you to be angry. You can be angry if you want. If that's what drives you, that's cool, go ahead and be angry. But this is just a convenient acronym for Moats, Affordance, and Diffusion, which are three characteristics or three pillars of a strategy for building on top of the models. First on Moats. Just for fun, anybody remember this slide from last year? One person, and he's my partner. Okay, that's cool. We're not going to... okay. As a reminder, this slide shows the merchandising cycle which is the links in the value chain required to take something from an idea to a happy customer. We're not actually going to go through the links in the chain. The point that I want to make here: if you approach things from a tech-out point of view, each link in the chain gets approached a little bit different. If you approach things from a customer-back point of view, each link in the chain you approach a little bit different. Now here's a part that's counter-intuitive. In a revolution of computation, which is about information processing, what you want to do is look down here because there's cool new stuff coming out all the time. What you should actually do for the sake of building moats is look up here because your customers are not changing nearly as fast as the capabilities are changing. The things that you build might be irrelevant tomorrow. The degree to which you wrap yourself around your customers is going to be a bit more durable. That's not to say that product and technology is not important. It is insanely important and generally speaking best product wins. But in a world where product changes so fast because capabilities change so fast, in thinking about moats we would encourage you to go as customer-back as possible and think about all the ways you can wrap yourself around those customers. Okay, the A in MAD stands for affordance. This is a term that we borrow from the design world. A hammer is an object that has affordance. I have a two-year old son. If I gave him a hammer, he would know what to do with it. He would grab it and start hitting stuff. That's why we don't give him hammers, okay? An object with affordance is one that doesn't need to be explained. People just know what to do with it. Claude Code is insanely powerful. Go open up a terminal for the average Fortune 500 employee and see how far they get. While it is powerful, it does not offer that much affordance. That's not a knock on Anthropic. But it is an opportunity for anybody who wants to build on top and to create paths of least resistance for your specific customers and their specific problems so that it's just brain-dead simple for them to figure out how to get to the outcome that they need for their business. That's the concept of affordance. And then finally the D in MAD is diffusion. And the diffusion gap is the opportunity for companies building at the application layer. The rate at which capabilities are diffusing out into the market is far shy of the rate at which those capabilities are being created. And every day that the foundation models move faster than your average Fortune 500 enterprise, that gap gets bigger and that opportunity gets bigger. So for moats, try to think customer-back. For affordance, try to think about creating those paths of least resistance for your customers. And that diffusion gap, that represents your opportunity. And lest that slide from earlier with the whitespace starting to fill up was discouraging for anybody, may we remind you that no lead is safe. There's this expression in racing: you cannot pass 15 cars in the sun, but you can pass 15 cars in the rain. And right now there is a torrential downpour of new capabilities coming out of the foundation models, which means that no lead is safe but it also means that anybody can win. What a time to be alive! And with that, I'll hand it off to Sonya. Thank you Pat. And can I just say it's so nice to see so many friendly faces in the audience. There is an exceptional group of people here today and I'm just really happy to be part of this ecosystem with all of you. The purpose of my section is to talk about what's happening in AI right now, which for 2026 is agents. Okay, flashback to 2022. Show of hands, does anybody here remember Auto-GPT or BabyAGI? Okay. So these projects were overnight hits on GitHub and what they did was they took GPT-3, gave it some tools, wrapped it in a loop, and let it run towards a goal. And it was promising until you watch those agents just fail over and over and over again. Kind of cute, kind of endearing, but completely useless. And I put this slide here to remind us that, you know, we all knew agents were coming. We could have seen it years ago. But back in 2022, the models just weren't ready yet. Fast forward to today. Something around the turn of the year really changed. Suddenly we have agents everywhere around us and they seem to actually be working. Two agents in particular have been home runs. Claude Code for the technical crowd and OpenClaw and all of its lobster brethren, which democratized agents to anybody with a phone. And so whether you are a hardcore engineer or a normie, the punchline is that anybody can create agents now. And so what we're seeing is people are building agents for everything. There is silly stuff like an OpenClaw agent that will literally snitch on your neighbors for tax fraud. Please don't do this—or actually, maybe please do this. There's entrepreneurial stuff—agents running generative media campaigns to sell construction services. And then there's the professional layer. I can tell you there's a huge race internally at Sequoia for who can build the best agents to do our jobs better. So what does it mean to be an agent? Here is one possible definition: an agent is a system that perceives its environment, chooses actions, and progresses autonomously towards a goal. By the way guys, I made this in SeaDance by myself, I'm very proud of it. The video models have come a long way. And more specifically I view agents as having three functional components. First is the ability to reason and plan. This is the baseline level of intuition and the ability to think on the fly. Second is the ability to take actions. This is tools: search, write, compile. And then finally, the ability to iterate towards a goal. This is the persistence that gives agents the ability to accomplish things over long time horizons. And so agency combines these three things. It is simply the ability to get shit done. How did we get here? If we boil agents down to their constituent components—the models, the tools, the harnesses—each component has progressed rapidly over the last year. First the models are the brain. This is the most important thing that's happened. The METR chart measures how long a model can sustain progress on a complex task without going off the rails. And we've gone from the order of tens of minutes a year ago to the order of hours today. And so this is the most important thing that's happened. The models are finally getting capable enough to sustain performance on long-horizon tasks. Second, the tools are the arms and the legs. These give models access to things that make us productive on a computer: the terminal for file systems and dev tools, iMessage, Slack, web search, computer use, you name it. And the last two decades that we spent building tools for humans have ended up being able to port over to be incredibly useful for agents as well. There's a common refrain that SaaS is dead. I think to the contrary, the value of these tools is going to explode as the number of agents using them increases. Models and tools give agents capability. The harness is what gives them persistence: the ability to stay on task, adapt, and keep going. And that feedback loop is now really starting to crank. Especially now with reinforcement learning, we're taking them to driving school, training them in RL gyms. We're pushing performance in different settings from mechanical engineering to design to finance. We're also seeing the early glimmers of self-improvement, or the machine building the machine. For example, Andrej's other research project improves research autonomously towards a GPT-2 level model in just two hours. So what does a world of agents everywhere look like? Agents exist on a sliding scale of agentic-ness. Let's take coding as an example. In 2023 we had tab auto-complete. One AI assisting a human inline. This was incrementally useful, fundamentally not transformative. We now have agentic development. One human talking to an agent, instructing it what to do, maybe managing a team of agents. But this paradigm is getting pushed further. We're now seeing background agents, async agents, agents spawning sub-agents. We think that async agents and this whole paradigm is likely to overtake the current paradigm in value, just because of the leverage in the system. And then finally, pushing the bleeding edge of the frontier, what I call dark factories. Taking human review out of the system completely. This sounds crazy, but I've seen it happen in production, including with cybersecurity companies. It is possible with good enough guardrails and good enough engineering. So we're progressing up a scale of agentic-ness. Agents are going from little helpers that do a little amount by your side, to interns that need to be managed, to interns that manage themselves, and eventually to interns that can be trusted enough to push to prod without oversight. And so that's the evolution that's happening, not just in coding but across all of agents. The most important takeaway for the founders in this room is that services is the new software. Pat's been saying this for as long as I've known him. Our partner Julian, who's in the audience today as well, published a great article on this. And we've known this for a long time, but I think it's actually happening. So in medicine, you're going to be able to hire an agent that inspects your genome, gives you personalized recommendations, can prescribe you medication, or recommend you clinical trials. In law, you'll be able to hire agents that negotiate contracts on your behalf, even perform litigation and settle it for you. In math and the sciences, we're seeing agents that can solve Erdos problems or discover new superconductors. Like, how thrilling is that? In the consumer world, personal agents that can manage your inbox for you, your calendar, your finances, and file your taxes. And we expect there's going to be agents everywhere. And that's in part because hiring agents is so much easier than hiring employees. Humans are hard to scale. Agents are infinitely scalable with compute. Humans are hard to keep happy. Except for me, I'm always happy. Agents are low maintenance. Humans are expensive, you pay them salaries. You pay agents tokens. Generally it costs less to accomplish a task with tokens than the equivalent in salary. Today humans are still generally smarter, but the bitter lesson presses on and soon agents will be smarter at many things. And so the point of this slide is not that we humans are out of a job. I think a uniquely human trait is adaptability. But we do expect the deployment of agents across the application layer to be swift and at an unprecedented rate and scale because the economics are so clear and because of the inherent scalability of bits. So if you add all this up, the number of agents is ballooning on some sort of exponential, maybe super-exponential, and I think we're about to hit the point where things get genuinely strange. What happens when commerce happens between agents? Can they pay each other? What happens when agents can actually negotiate the terms of a transaction with each other? Are we going to have swarms of agents policing us, preventing things like cybersecurity Armageddon? All we know is the world is getting weird extremely quickly. And so I'll close by channeling my inner Bene Gesserit. Long-horizon agents are here. The curve that they're on is very clear. And for founders, I think everybody has examples of people that are accomplishing insanely hard timelines thanks to AI. The Notion team rewrote eight million lines of code in just six weeks. So everybody has these examples of compressed timelines. But I think very few people outside of the AGI labs have seen what happens when you take these compressed timelines and you stack them on top of each other. And that's what's possible now. And so whatever you could imagine building over the next hundred years, we think is now possible in a hundred days thanks to agents. I will pass over to Konstantine. Thank you Sonya. Alright, thank you so much Sonya, Pat, for the brilliant overview analysis. In this section we're going to talk a little bit about what's next. So the goal here is, we all know we're in the AI age. What's it going to look like? What's it going to feel like? How is it characterized? Earlier in the presentation Pat bifurcated technological revolutions between compute and communication. We're going to do another bifurcation here for types of work. There is physical work. This is a package on the Pony Express, this is a satellite on a Falcon 9. Work equals force times distance, physical movement. And then there's cognitive work. This is Pythagoras coming up with his theorem, this is DeepMind solving the protein folding problem. Conscious thinking. These are very different types of work. But we believe that they're going to follow a very similar pattern in revolution. So let's talk about physical work because we've been through this revolution with the Industrial Revolution. For the vast majority of human history, all the work, or virtually all the work for serving humans was done by some sort of muscle. People or animals. People moving something or an animal pulling the human along. This starts at 1700 but it goes back millennia. Then things started to change. Water and wind, steam engines. And then things accelerated. Steam engines, combustion, electric motors. Today, 2026, you could estimate that 99 plus percent of all the physical work done on planet Earth for humans is done by a machine. The plane that brought you here, the manufacturing of all the goods in this room, all the transportation that sets up for the pinnacle of the human experience you're having right now. Well, we think a similar pattern is going to happen in cognition. We're just a little earlier on. For most of human history, all the thinking on planet Earth for humans was done primarily by humans, maybe a little bit for animals—the sheepdog chasing the sheep, right? And there was this sliver on top of mechanical work, the astrolabe or the clock. Now over the past couple hundred years there was not a lot of progress until electronic computation. And in the past hundred years, think about all the trillions of calculations that are happening at any given moment to serve you the human. All of that work, all of that cognitive work that's happening to serve us at any given moment. Trillions of calculations. We believe that the neural network is the next big wave and that in the near future 99.9 percent of cognition on planet Earth will be done by machines. Well, the parallel is pretty stark. And the good news is we've been through a revolution like this. The cognitive revolution is going to be a lot like the industrial revolution, just much, much bigger and much faster. So what's it going to be like living in this future? I'd like to share some motivations for this future in the form of four short stories. In the mid-1800s America wanted to build a grand monument to our first president and our greatest war hero, George Washington. So we designed the tallest building in the world at the time, the Washington National Monument, and we wanted to cap it with the most precious metal in the world. 100 ounces of the most precious metal in the world. So precious, in fact, that we put it on display at Tiffany's in Manhattan. That metal was aluminum. Within decades of the completion of the Washington National Monument, a young inventor came up with electrolysis, the process of separating aluminum from dirt. And within decades, aluminum was used to wrap our candies and our sandwiches and then tossed into the trash. Aluminum is intelligence. Electrolysis is artificial intelligence. We're about to enter a world where some of the most precious skills that took decades to earn—PhD-level skills—are so instantly invoked that right after using them, you can crumple them up and throw them right in the trash. We are entering a world of alien design. The world as we see it today is all about design for humans. You know, it's been optimized in a way that makes sense to our brains because we are doing almost all the cognition in the world. Well, when machines do the cognition it's going to be a little different. In 2006, NASA was optimizing an antenna for a large space mission, a satellite space mission. And traditionally their antennas looked like this. It was a beautiful geometric, symmetrical pattern that optimized surface area for some power constraints. This time around they said we're going to hand it over to a computer and we're going to have an evolutionary algorithm, a lot like reinforcement learning. The result: this antenna right here. Dramatically more productive, not intuitive to the human mind. In this AI era when we hand over cognition to machines, we're going to get results that are not intuitive to us. When AI is designing chips, cars, buildings, they might look dramatically different. The world that we enter into, we have to be open-minded because the AI is not going to think like us. It's going to have alien design. The third motivation story is on emerging sciences. Not emerging science, we all know there's emerging science. I'm talking about emerging sciences. In the early industrial revolution, you had great engineers like Newcomen and Watt and they perfected combustion engines. Basically put a petrochemical into a piston, ignite it on fire, millions, billions of particles explode, move the piston, work. For almost a hundred years all of that was tinkering. It was an engineer saying, "that works a little bit better", maybe something you could see like a scaling law, but it was engineers playing with the product and seeing how they could improve it a little bit. Over 120 years after, Sadi Carnot came around and formalized this in a new science—thermodynamics. He said, "Wait a second, there are millions or billions of particles, we can actually formalize what that all looks like." In this case, there are billions of neurons, trillions of tokens. Right now we're in the tinkering phase of AI. Even if we think it's an understood science, it is not. In the future we will have a science as fundamental as thermodynamics introduced in the next couple decades. Someone in this room might come up with that science. And that science will be taught in high schools. It will be that fundamental. And it will help us master AI, it will even help us master consciousness. The Art of Unreason. So for the vast majority of human history, tens of thousands of years, art has been a progression towards realism. This is a cave painting from about 25,000 years ago, Egyptian hieroglyphs, Greek pottery, Renaissance paintings—a grand transformation towards realistic art. Just look at the difference over tens of thousands of years, the triumph of humanity. And then, engineering came along. The daguerreotype, early photography. And all of a sudden what was spent decades of life to perfect the skill of getting every brushstroke perfect, done. So how did the world react? They thought that painting was over. "Oh, that's it, the machine can do it better than any human, art is ended." Well, what happened? How did humans respond? Humans responded by saying, "Was the purpose of this art to capture the moment in the way the eye sees it, or was it to capture the moment in the way the heart and the soul sees it?" Impressionism, expressionism, cubism, neo-expressionism. All these new forms of art are how humanity responded to this dramatic change in science. 2500 years ago Greek philosopher Protagoras wrote, "Man is the measure of all things." What he meant is that nothing in a vacuum has value to humans. Not aluminum, not art, not intelligence. It only has value because of the experience. AI can do the work, AI will do the work. But only the human connection can give you a reason to care. That's why we're all in this room today. A decade from now work is going to be dramatically different. Things are going to change so much. But the one thing that will be constant is the relationships that you form today with the person right next to you will endure. That's what you're going to look back on. That's what's going to be valuable from today. So I encourage you to form those relationships with the people next to you. Enjoy your time together at this AI Ascent and really lean into what makes us most human.

Former NEC Director Gary Cohn: Rising oil prices will have an impact on U.S. consumption power

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·11:01 ·14.8K views · 2026-04-30
Gary Cohn (Vice Chairman, IBM)
The U.S.
TL;DR The U.S. economy is currently buoyed by a massive $1 trillion infrastructure and AI CAPEX boom that is offsetting the inflationary pressure of high oil prices. While high energy costs are depleting consumer disposable income, the physical build-out of data centers and...

TL;DR

The U.S. economy is currently buoyed by a massive $1 trillion infrastructure and AI CAPEX boom that is offsetting the inflationary pressure of high oil prices. While high energy costs are depleting consumer disposable income, the physical build-out of data centers and domestic energy independence provide a unique structural advantage over global competitors.

The Physical Reality of the AI CAPEX Boom

  • The "Caterpillar" Indicator: Gary Cohn argues that the nearly $1 trillion in planned CAPEX for data centers translates directly into demand for heavy machinery.
      • Infrastructure Foundation: Building data centers requires moving dirt, laying foundations, and lifting steel—processes that rely on companies like Caterpillar.
      • Policy Tailwinds: The ability for companies to write off and depreciate equipment quickly (100% expensing) is a significant driver of this incremental machine buying.
  • Three Pillars of Earnings: Cohn identifies the primary drivers of current economic resilience as:

1. AI tech expansion

2. GLP-1 (weight loss drug) development

3. Physical data center construction

Oil Prices and the American Consumer

  • Consumption Power: Rising oil prices are acting as a direct tax on the American worker, particularly those who commute 50–100 miles daily.
      • Disposable Income: Cohn emphasizes that consumers feel the immediate impact at the pump, which reduces spending power elsewhere in the economy.
  • U.S. Energy Independence: Despite high prices, the U.S. remains in a superior position compared to Europe or Asia.
      • Supply Security: Unlike international peers who face potential fuel shortages or business shutdowns, the U.S. has no supply problem; the domestic debate is strictly about price, not availability.
  • Economic Tug-of-War: Cohn notes the truth of the economy lies between two extremes: the "six weeks to a problem" bear view and the "growth will outweigh everything" bull view.

AI Regulation and Anthropic’s Mythos

  • Government Scrutiny: Cohn addresses reports that the White House is concerned about Anthropic’s new "Mythos" model due to its immense power.
      • Cybersecurity & Compute: The primary concerns in Washington involve the cybersecurity risks associated with such a powerful model and whether there is sufficient compute power for both government and private sector use.
  • Policy Intent: Cohn dismisses "cynical" views that the administration is trying to slow Anthropic down to let competitors like OpenAI or Google catch up. He asserts the government is genuinely trying to understand the resource and avoid unintended consequences.

Federal Reserve Internal Dynamics

  • Institutional Dissent: Cohn points out the rare level of friction within the Fed, noting that four dissenters is a level of internal disagreement not seen since the 1990s.
  • Kevin Warsh's Potential Role: Cohn praises Kevin Warsh as "unbelievably well-equipped" to lead, citing his critical experience at the Fed during the 2008 financial crisis.
  • Jay Powell’s Tenure: Cohn describes Powell's current stance as "untraditional," suggesting he is breaking from his usual pragmatic/traditionalist mold by staying in his seat despite external pressures and ongoing investigations by the Fed's Inspector General.
      • Short-term Outlook: Cohn predicts Powell will not stay until 2028, suggesting an earlier exit would be in the best interest of Fed independence and productivity gains.

The global order is approaching a high-risk "brink" driven by unsustainable U.S. debt and a shift toward "power-rules" geopolitics, while the stability of the public stock market increasingly hinges on Big Tech’s AI returns and the eventual transparency of OpenAI’s financials.

The Five Forces of the "Big Cycle"

Ray Dalio, founder of Bridgewater Associates, outlines a framework for understanding current market volatility through five interconnected drivers:

  • The Debt Cycle: Dalio characterizes the credit system as a "circulatory system."
      • Debt Plaque: When debt service rises faster than income, it acts like "plaque," squeezing out spending and creating supply-demand imbalances for government bonds.
      • U.S. Deficit Crisis: The U.S. national debt has surpassed $39 trillion, with the government spending 40% more than it earns ($7 trillion in spending vs. $5 trillion in revenue).
  • Internal Order: Increasing polarization between the political left and right, as well as wealth gaps between the rich and poor, are fraying the domestic social fabric.
  • Geopolitical Order: The world has shifted from the post-1945 multilateral order (UN, WTO, World Bank) to a "power rules" era.
      • Global Conflicts: Ongoing tensions involving Iran, China, and Russia are not isolated events but part of a broader "world war" dynamic with distinct sides.
  • Technology and Nature: AI is a revolutionary but potentially "bubbly" force, while natural disasters (pandemics, floods) historically disrupt the order more than kinetic wars.

Timing the Brink: 2026–2028

Dalio warns that we are currently in a "particularly risky period" with a confluence of threats peaking in the near term:

  • The Critical Window: The period between the 2026 midterms and the 2028 presidential election is identified as the highest risk for systemic conflict.
  • Monetary Threats: Demand for U.S. debt is falling as foreign holders (such as China) fear potential sanctions on dollar-denominated assets.
  • Political Gridlock: Dalio anticipates the 2026 elections could lead to a Republican loss of the House, resulting in intensified domestic conflict, including impeachments and investigations.

Strategic Portfolio Management

Given the macro environment, Dalio and host Ed Elson discuss how investors can protect capital:

  • Diversification over Market Timing: Individual investors should avoid timing markets based on headlines and instead build "all-weather" portfolios.
  • The Role of Gold: Gold should comprise 5% to 15% of a portfolio as a diversifier against debt crises and international conflict. It currently ranks as the second-largest reserve currency for central banks.
  • The Cash Trap: Cash is viewed as a "guaranteed bad investment" during periods of stagflation because it offers lower returns than rising inflation.
  • Geographic Spread: Investors must look outside the U.S. to mitigate the risks associated with domestic debt and polarization.

Big Tech and the AI ROI Debate

Gil Luria of D.A. Davidson analyzes recent earnings from the "Hyperscalers" to determine if massive AI investments are paying off:

  • Growth Metrics: Cloud revenue remains explosive, with Microsoft Azure up 40%, Amazon AWS up 27%, and Google Cloud up 63%.
  • The "Meta" Exception: Unlike Microsoft and Google, Meta is increasing CapEx without showing a corresponding acceleration in revenue, leading to investor skepticism.
  • Valuation Discrepancy: Microsoft trades at a forward multiple in the low 20s, which Luria views as an attractive entry point compared to Google and Amazon (high 20s/30s).
  • Insulation from Geopolitics: Big Tech remains largely insulated from Middle East tensions because oil is not a direct input; their primary driver is the demand for AI compute.

OpenAI as a Systemic Risk

Despite being a private company, OpenAI has become "structurally critical" to the public markets:

  • Market Sensitivity: A single report of OpenAI missing revenue targets recently erased $400 billion in market value from public companies like Nvidia, Oracle, and SoftBank.
  • The IPO Test: OpenAI’s planned public debut later this year is viewed as a "trillion-dollar question."
      • The Risk: If OpenAI’s quarterly disclosures reveal flaws in the AI thesis, the broader stock market could "start to come apart."
